NIGERIA
NUCLEUS ESTATE/SMALLHOLDER OIL PALM PROJECT
RIVERS STATE
Summary and Conclusions
i.        The Federal Military Government of Nigeria (FMG) has requested
Bank assistance to finance an oil palm project in Rivers State. This report
appraises a nucleus estate/smallholder project for which a US$30.0 million
loan is proposed.
ii.       The rapid growth in the Nigerian economy in recent years has to a
large extent bypassed the agricultural sector. In fact, in some respects
the boom elsewhere in the economy has made life more difficult for the rural
producer, for instance by the rapid bidding up of wage rates. It has become
increasingly clear, however, that the stagnation in the agricultural sector
cannot be allowed to continue. In the domestic market, the rapid growth in
purchasing power has resulted in price escalation and an increasing import
bill for foodstuffs (up from N50 million in 1970 to N440 million in 1976).
In the international context, Nigeria must in the longer term rebuild the
export capacity of her agricultural sector, the decline of which has been
temporarily masked by the oil boom. For palm oil, the target for the con-
ceivable future can only be to reduce domestic shortages and Nigeria's im-
pending dependence upon foreign supplies. According to estimates made in this
report, domestic demand for palm oil in 1985 will be in the region of 780,000
to 830,000 metric tons per annum, while the supply capability will not exceed
640,000 tons. An import gap of 140,000 to 190,000 tons is therefore projected,
even after taking account of the three Bank projects already approved.
iii.      The present project would plant 10,000 ha on a nucleus estate and
10,000 ha on smallholdings in the Elele-Ahoada area. The planting program
would be carried out over the period 1978-1982. The smallholder program
would be implemented by a Smallholder Management Unit (SMU) to be established
under the Rivers State Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources (MANR).
A grant/credit package in cash and kind to assist in meeting establishment
and maintenance costs would be channeled to smallholders by the SMU, working
in conjunction with local cooperative societies. The SMU would also operate
an extension service to provide technical advice to smallholders. In addition
funds for staff training are included in the project. A roads units, to be
established within the SMU, would be responsible for upgrading 400 km of roads
within the project area. The State-owned company RISONPALM Ltd. would estab-
lish and operate the nucleus estate, construct and operate two 40-ton ffb/hour
palm oil mills (one of which would be funded under the Bank Loan) and estab-
lish a fruit collection system capable of handling the production of both the
nucleus estate and the smallholders.



- ii -
iv.       The project includes a small component which would pave the way
for future development of coconut palms in southern Nigeria. Funding is
provided for a study by consultants of the region's potential for coconut and,
subject to the finding of this study, the project would support the establish-
ment of 120 ha seed garden to provide high yielding hybrid planting material
for future development projects. Funds for a feasibility study of a Phase
II Oil Palm Development are also included in the project.
v.        Project costs over the six and a half year development period
(1978-84) are estimated at N54.0 million (US$83.0 million). The Bank loan
of N 19.4 million (US$30.0 million) would finance 36% of total project costs;
it would be equivalent to all foreign exchange costs, including those incurred
in 1977/1978 (regarded as a start-up year). The remainder of the project costs
would be financed by the Federal Military Government N 13.3 million (US$20.5
million) or 25%, by the Rivers State Government N 20.1 million (US$30.9
million) or 37%, and by participating smallholders N 1.2 million (US$1.8
million) or 2%.
vi.       Vehicles, tractors, fertilizers, chemicals, road-making equipment,
other equipment and farm inputs valued at about N10.3 million (US$15.8 million)
net of contingencies, would be procured through international competitive
bidding in accordance with Bank guidelines for those contracts valued over
US$75,000, and through competitive bidding and in accordance with local proce-
dures satisfactory to the Bank for contracts less than US$75,000. For contracts
under US$10,000, direct competitive shopping would be employed. Domestically
manufactured goods would be allowed a 15% preference or the applicable import
duty, whichever is lower. Contracts for houses, stores and buildings, which
are valued at N6.0 million (US$9.2 million), would be awarded on the basis of
local competitive bidding. Construction of roads would be undertaken by the
project on force account. A laLrge part of project cost would be salaries,
operating expenses, training costs and field establishment costs, valued at
N16.9 million (US$26.0 million) which are unsuitable for competitive bidding.
The services of consultants and expatriate staff valued at N2.3 million
(US$3.5 million) would be obtained in accordance with procedures acceptable
to the Bank. An amount of N18.5 million (US$28.5 million) would cover
contingencies.
vii.      The Bank loan would b,e disbursed over a six and a half year period
1978-1984 as follows: (1) Nucleus Estate (a) 100% foreign exchange costs of
field establishment (excluding labor, locally procured seeds and cover crops,
and land acquisition), housing, civil works, offices, stores, and other build-
ings, or (if locally procured) 35% of these expenditures, totalling US$8.1
million; (b) 100% of foreign exchange costs of vehicles and equipment, in-
ternational staff salaries, consultants, feasibility study, training, man-
agement fee and operating costs or (if locally procured) 75% of these costs,
amounting to US$12.8 million; (2) Coconut Seed Garden, 100% of the foreign
exchange costs of the coconut seed garden or (if locally procured) 45% of



- iii -
these expenditures, totalling US$0.8 million; (3) SMU - (a) 100% of foreign
exchange costs of field establishment (excluding labor, locally procured
seeds and cover crop) and housing or (if locally procured) 50% of these
costs totalling US$1.9 million; (b) 100% of foreign exchange costs of road
construction (excluding local staff salaries), staff training, international
staff salaries, vehicles and equipment, consultants and operating costs or
(if locally procured) 30% of these expenditures totalling US$3.7 million.
Retroactive financing of up to US$1.25 million is proposed for recruitment
of international staff and purchase of land clearing equipment. Disburse-
ment of the Bank loan would be against import documentation, contracts and
certificates of expenditure: the latter would be scruitinized by Bank super-
vision missions and independent auditors.
viii.      At full maturity in 1990 the combined estate and smallholder project
would produce annually about 47,250 metric tons of palm oil for domestic
consumption and 10,126 metric tons of kernels for export. Valued at the
Bank's price forecasts for world markets in 1985, the annual foreign exchange
value of output would be US$23.0 million (constant 1977 terms).
ix.       Based on a fresh fruit bunch price of N40 per ton, smallholder
gross return (including return to labor input) from a 2 hectare planting
would start at N254 in the first year of yielding (four years after planting),
rising to N578 per annum over the farmer's loan repayment period, and there-
after to N814 a year. The gross return per manday of labor input would rise
from N3.9 in the year of first production, to N8.6 during loan repayment,
and an eventual N12.9. These returns compare favorably with local wage
rates of about N2.0 per manday.
x.        During the establishment period the project would create 18,750
man years of employment. At full maturity the project would provide wage
and salaried employment for about 2,000 people. Based on an average farm
size of 2 ha about 5,000 farmers would benefit. The average family size
is 7, so about 35,000 family members would be direct beneficiaries from
the smallholder component.
xi.       The estimated economic rate of return is 15.2%.
xii.      On the basis of the assurances obtained at negotiations and set
out in Chapter IX, the project is suitable for a US$30.0 million Bank loan,
for a period of 20 years including a five-year grace period on principal.






I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Federal Military Government of Nigeria (FMG) has requested
a Bank loan to help finance planting of high yielding oil palms in Rivers
State. This report appraises a project that would establish 10,000 ha of
nucleus estate and 10,000 ha of smallholder plantings as Phase I of a program
that would eventually establish 40,000 ha of high yielding palms.
1.02      The first Bank loans for agriculture in Nigeria were for the
Western State Cocoa Project (1971, Loan 764-UNI, US$7.2 million), now com-
pleted, and the Second Cocoa Project (1974, Loan 1045-UNI, US$20 million),
which is being successfully implemented in the former Western and Mid-Western
States 1/. Following an identification mission in 1972 loans were made for
three integrated agricultural development projects in the north: Funtua
Project (1974, Loan 1092-UNI, US$29 million), Gusau Project (1974, Loan
1099-UNI, US$19 million), and Gombe Project (1974, Loan 1164-UNI, US$21 mil-
lion); a Rice Project (1974, Loan 1103-UNI, US$17.5 million) in the former
East Central and South East States 2/; a Livestock Project (1974, Loan 1091-
UNI, US$21 million); and three Oil Palm Projects; Nucleus Estate/Smallholder
Oil Palm Project, Western State 3/ (1975, Loan 1192-UNI, US$17 million),
Nucleus Estate/Smallholder Oil Palm Project, Mid-Western State 4/ (1975, Loan
1183-UNI, US$29.5 million) and Smallholder Oil Palm Project, East Central
State 5/ (1975, Loan 1191-UNI, US$19 million). The northern agricultural
development projects are being implemented successfully; the remaining proj-
ects have experienced delays but are expected to make up lost time as manage-
ment gains experience. Two further agricultural development projects in Benue
and Plateau States recently became effective (Ayangba: 1977, Loan 1455-,UNI,
US$35 million, Lafia:  1977, Loan 1454-UNI, US$27 million).   Most of the
projects are encountering substantial cost overruns due to rapid internal
inflation, and supply of local funds to existing projects already represents a
serious problem. Currently under appraisal are two more agricultural develop-
ment projects (Bida and Ilorin), a forestry project and a project to establish
an Agricultural and Rural Management Training Institute.
1.03      The project was identified by the Federal Department of Agriculture
and the Resident Mission in Lagos in May 1975, and subsequently prepared by a
consultant firm SOCFINCO Ltd, engaged by the Ministry of Trade and Economic
1/   Following the creation of the new states, the project is now executed
in Oyo, Ogun, Ondo and Bendel States, and project assets and liabilities
are being divided accordingly.
2/   Now in three States:  Anambra, Imo and Cross River.
3/   Now in Ondo State.
4/   Now in Bendel State.
5/   Now in Imo State.



- 2--_
Development. The feasibility studies were completed in December 1975 and
April 1976 and the project was appraised in October/November 1976 by Messrs.
Bevan, Hachero and Ody (Bank), Bogaerts, Hallgrimson and Olivin (Consultants).
II. BACKGROUND
The Economy
2.01      Following the recent creation of seven new states, the Federal 2
Republic of Nigeria comprises L9 States.  It covers an area of 924,000 km
and has an estimated population of 77 million. The skewness in income dis-
tribution means that the majority of the population have incomes below the
estimated national average of US$380 (1976), particularly in rural areas,
where economic activities are based primarily on smallholder farming with low
level technology. In urban centers, average incomes are higher but unequally
distributed.
2.02      Economic growth in Nigeria over the last six years has occurred in
two phases: from January 1970 until the end of 1972 was a period of rehabili-
tation and, with the economy responding to pent-up demand, postponed invest-
ment and production were quickly realized. Following a year of consolidation
and slower growth in 1972, the second phase was characterized by a resumption
of growth generated by increased public sector activities. This was accom-
panied by inflation, supply shortages and infrastructural congestion as excess
capacity diminished. Although primary sector performance was disappointing,
the economy continued to grow rapidly because of the phenomenal expansion of
the service sectors; from 1970-76 GDP rose at an average rate of 7.4% per
annum.  Nigeria's economic development has been increasingly dominated by
oil, which by 1976 accounted for 31% for GDP, 93% of export earnings and
82% of total Government revenues, surpassing the agricultural sector in all
areas except employment. The Third National Development Plan covers the
five-year period April 1975-March 1980. Revised plan allocations are US$68
billion, but due to slippage the effective program is projected at US$45
billion. The plan has been reviewed by the new Government and more emphasis
has been given to health, housing and agriculture.
The Agricultural Sector
2.03      General.  Approximately 61% of Nigeria's population is dependent
on agriculture which accounts for about 23% of GDP, 5% of total export
earnings and 62% of non-oil exports. Agricultural production is almost
entirely in the hands of smallholders whose levels of technology and
productive efficiency are low.
2.04      Cropping patterns vary within the country's main ecological zones.
In the forest zones of the South, where rainfall is bimodally distributed over
a wet season of up to 8 months, the agricultural economy is largely based on
tree crops with annual staples providing subsistence and subsidiary income.



- 3 -
In the Guinea and Sudan Savannah zones of the north, rainfall is monomodal
within a six-month spread and production is based almost entirely on annual
crops. Between these two zones is an area known as the "middle belt" support-
ing southern Guinea and transitional forest savannah vegetation and a mix of
annual and tree crops. Little development has occurred in this "middle belt"
which forms an important food production reserve for the nation.
2.05      Although general economic growth has been high, agricultural
sector performance has been disappointing, with average annual growth in
1970-1976 of only 0.9%, less than the population increase. Serious drought
conditions in the north led to a sharp fall in output in 1972 and 1973;
production of millet and sorghum, major northern food crops, fell 15% in
1973, and groundnut production fell by 35% between 1972 and 1974. Millet
and sorghum output recovered significantly due to favorable weather condi-
tions in 1974 and 1975, but groundnuts continued to suffer from disease
and other problems. Growth is still impeded by the low level of technology,
declining productivity on existing farms and substantial migration of the
rural labor force to urban centers. Nevertheless, agriculture provides
employment for three quarters of the labor force. The share of agriculture
in GDP (in current terms) declined from 49% in 1970 to 23% in 1976.
2.06      Food crop production over the last decade has not kept pace with
demand, resulting in increased food prices and a rise in imports (from N50
million in 1970 to around N440 million in 1976). Agricultural exports are
equally unsatisfactory, suffering from increased demand in the domestic market,
the present level of the exchange rate, and the failure until recently of the
monopsonist marketing boards to offer adequate price incentives for export.
Thus, although cocoa continues to be an important export, production remains at
the level of the early 1960's. In the case of groundnuts, falling production
combined with increasing domestic demand has eliminated exports altogether.
2.07      Although palm oil production has risen since the civil war, it has
not yet recovered to pre-war levels. Rising population and incomes have led
to a buoyant local demand and soaring prices, and no significant quantities of
palm oil have been exported for several years. The Bank is already assisting
in the development of three oil palm projects in Nigeria (loans 1183-UNI;
1191-UNI; and 1192-UNI), which in 1985 will contribute about 32,000 tons
of palm oil per annum increasing to 100,000 tons by 1990. Nonetheless, the
prospect for the next 10-15 years is of a widening gap between demand and
supply. It is projected that by 1985, the shortfall will amount to 140,000-
190,000 tons per annum (see Annex 1).
2.08      Development Strategy.  Government is aware of the need to improve
performance in the agricultural sector and to invest the substantial revenues
from oil t-) expand and diversify the rest of the ecoromy. In the revised
Third-Five-Year Deve]opmenL Plan, N3.0 billion has been earmarked for
agriculture to support a targeted growth rate of 5%. The program includes
development of several major river basins for irrigation, dissemination of
modern farmiing techniques to smallholders, improved input distribution and
marketing services and support for various integrated development projects.
Rural development will be promoted with complementary investments in



- 4 -
secondary and feeder roads, rural electrification, health and other social
services.
2.09      Government's main short-term objective is to improve internal price
stability in basic food crops and to ensure adequate food supplies to the
rapidly expanding urban centers. To complement this, Government's longer-term
objectives are to create an efficient agricultural industry, to reduce popula-
tion drift to urban centers and to revive Nigeria's agricultural exports.
2.10      Development Constraints.  The main development constraint is the
lack of effective agricultural support services. Produce evacuation is ham-
pered by lack of feeder roads, and many farmers have to travel long distances
to unevenly distributed and unreliable sources of inputs.
2.11      Input demand, at the present heavily subsidized prices, is high and
as a result of supply problems black market activities are common. However,
even in areas of high input consumption, yields are low and many farm opera-
tions are not viable in economic terms.
2.12      Rapid development in the non-agricultural sectors and rising stand-
ards of education have led to reaction against poor returns to labor and the
drudgery associated with traditional agriculture. This has encouraged the
drift of young people away from rural areas and has resulted in a sharp rise
in hired labor rates. This can only be reduced through encouraging applica-
tion of advanced farming techniques, which are particularly adapted to larger
farming units-either group or individually operated, as a complement to
support for smallholder development.
2.13      The Federal Government is generally aware of these constraints and
of the need for further improvement in planning and executive capabilities
at both State and Federal levels. Furthermore, there is a dearth of technical
and managerial personnel at all levels, which suggests a need for greater
rationalization of the responsibilities of public agencies and also much
closer coordination in the activities of such agencies, if the effectiveness
of available manpower is to be maximized.
2.14      Agricultural support services remain almost entirely in the public
sector, and present subsidy policies have removed the incentive for private
sector participation. Giveni Nigeria's size and massive development program,
Government will need progressively to divest itself of its responsibilities
for input-and produce-marketing to private enterprise; this would require
an appropriate economic framework and redefinition of the role of Federal
and State agricultural ministries.
2.15      Bank Role and Sector Lending Strategy.  Since 1971, the Bank has
been involved in 12 tree or food crop projects (see para. 1.02).  During
this time the success of the three northern projects (Loans 1092, 1099 and
1164-UNI) has demonstrated the advantages of planned agricultural develop-
ment implemented under a structure which allows State control while affording
management full operational autonomy. As a result, both Federal and State
Governments are anxious to replicate this experience elsewhere. There is
also increasing interest in the involvement of the commercial sector which
is being pursued under these projects.



- 5 -
2.16      Experience of implementing the ongoing projects has reinforced Bank
concern over a number of critical policy issues, resolution of which are vital
for medium and long-term sector development and which must be taken into con-
sideration in future Bank operations. First: subsidizing inputs (fertilizer,
insecticides, herbicides), irrigation water rates, tractor hire charges, in-
terest rates and other similar items is encouraging inefficient resource use,
distorting resource allocation, discouraging development of farm support ser-
vices by the private sector and straining Government's fiscal and adminis-
trative resources. It is estimated that at the current level of subsidies,
fertilizer subsidies alone in 1980 could amount, allowing for projected in-
creases in utilization, to about N100 million annually (about US$150 million).
Second: input procurement and distribution has been centralized at the
Federal level, resulting in inefficient supply to farmers and a needless
additional burden on the already overstrained agriculture public administra-
tion. Third: State and Federal funding has been unsatisfactory, creating
severe problems for Bank projects. Fourth: the combination of official
credit policies with the disappointing performance of NAB means that no
viable financial framework has been created for the development of either
smallholders' or commercial agriculture. Fifth: market intervention by the
commodity boards has not been fully effective in stabilizing iilernal prices
of agricultural commodities (particularly for the non-scheduled food crops),
maintaining price incentives to producers and encouraging a competitive mar-
keting infrastructure. These problems are further exacerbated by inadequate
rural feeder road systems. Finally, shortages of experienced manpower at
managerial, administrative and technical levels are major constraints in the
planning and implementation of development programs.
2.17      Resolving these issues is very difficult and Government has sought
Bank financial and technical support. For example, Government has infon ally
requested the Bank to review subsidy, interest rate and pricing policies and
the forthcoming Economic Mission will analyze the problems and make recom.nenda-
tions for future Bank lending. The provision of infrastructure comprising
roads, service centers, input distribution and improved extension systems
forms a major component of the Bank's existing and proposed agriculture devel-
opment projects. The short-term solution for the management and administra-
tion constraint adopted in Bank-supported projects in Nigeria is internation ?
recruitment and the establishment of special project management units. How-
ever, in the long-term, training of Nigerian management staff is essential
and Government has requested the Bank to consider financing an Agricultural
and Rural Management Training Institute. A proposal was prepared by consul-
tants, and the project was appraised in February/March 1977.
III. THE PROJECT AREA
3.01      The project area, which covers some 2,800 km  of the Rivers State,
includes the previous Ikwerre and Etche divisions and parts of Ahoada, Obio
and Ogba-Egbema divisions: these have now been incorporated in the new
Ahoada, Ikwerre-Etche and Port Harcourt City Locnl Government Council areas.



-6-
Climate
3.02      Rainfall distribution is characterized by a long rainy season from
early March to end November. Mean annual rainfall in Ahoada (situated in the
west) is 2,640 mm and the mean annual water deficit is estimated at 225 mm
(amongst the lowest in West Africa). Annual sunshine is estimated at 1,400-
1,500 hours.  Mean annual temperatures are 26  - 28 C and are good for oil
palm cultivation (see Maps IBRD 10561R1 and 10562R1).
Vegetation
3.03      There is considerable variation in vegetation.  In the east of the
nucleus estate, population density is low and vegetation consists mainly of
oil palm grove mixed with fallow; about 60% can be classified as forest rem-
nants or secondary jungle, with a high density of medium sized trees. In the
west of the estate, about 40% can be classified as forest remnants and sec-
ondary jungle, with wild palms varying between 50 and 150 trees/ha. All
recently cleared areas are heavily infested by Eupatorium Odoratum. 1/
Topography/Hydrology
3.04      Two basic geological foundations cover the project area:  the
Coastal Plain Terrace and the Sub-Recent Niger Terrace, the latter covering
most of the western sector. The Coastal Plain Terrace relief is only very
slightly undulating with gradual slopes to the south. A few depressions
accumulate water to form small swamps and the topography is broken by a few
large rivers. The lower altitude of the Sub-Recent Niger Terrace has a
network of small streams flowing into the tidal flats.
Soils
3.05      The soils of the Coastal Plain Terrace are deep with clay content
increasing with profile depth. They have a good structure, are free draining
and are suitable for oil palm cultivation. On the Sub-Recent Nigeria Terrace
only very limited areas of water logged or patches of very sandy soil are
unsuitable for oil palm cultivation.
Population
3.06      The project area population is estimated at between 240,000 a d
300,000 (see Map IBRD 12590) with average densities of 90 to 100 per km
Within the nucleus estate area, the density is low. The average nuclear
family size is 7, and about 70% derive the majority of their income from
agriculture or fishing. Agricultural work is seasonal, and during peak
periods there is some temporary inward migration of laborers from neighboring
states. Rural labor markets are well developed, and a significant proportion
of agricultural work is performed for money wages.
1/   A noxious weed which adversely affects oil palm establishment.



7-
Land Tenure
3.07      Generally land is vested in the various communities, transactions
being under the direct control of the community head in consultation with
chiefs and elders. Land may be given (though unrecorded) by the community
to an individual as his personal and disposable property. Alternatively,
land may be given by the community to a family 1/ under the provision that
the family head may transfer it outside the family only with the consent and
sanction of the community elders. Although title to land is generally un-
recorded, family and individual rights are usually well known and accepted
within the community. In general the traditional system provides adequate
security of tenure for planting permanent crops. There is increasing indi-
vidual ownership in some areas, especially close to towns and absentee owner-
ship is becoming common. Under the new Land Act enacted in April 1978, all
land is vested in the State. While it is not yet clear how the provisions will
operate in practice, it is most unlikely that they will adversely affect the
project: indeed implementation could well be facilitated. Under the project,
the selection process would be designed to restrict participation to those
directly engaged in farming (Annex 5 Appendix I).
3.08      The Public Lands Requisition Law provides for Government land
acquisition for public use. This may be by voluntary transfer or compulsory
acquisition with payment of reasonable compensation. Compulsory acquisition
generally takes place through gazetting and requires little time. Compensa-
tion for land acquired for the nucleus estate would be in the form of a down
payment and annual rental acceptable to the landowners and to the Bank. It
was a condition of Board presentation that at least 3,000 ha of land had
been acquired by the State and conveyed to RISONPALM on a lease basis of not
less than 50 years under conditions acceptable to the communities involved.
During negotiations assurances were obtained that the balance of 8,000 ha
would be acquired by the State and conveyed to RISONPALM, with all boundary
alignments agreed with local communities and final traces cut and clearly
marked not later than March 31, 1979. It was agreed during negotiations
that the acquisition and conveyance of the 8,000 ha would be a condition of
disbursement against estate development.
Farming Systems
3.09      Farm sizes in the area vary considerably, but according to a recent
unofficial survey, the average family has about 0.7 ha under food crops at any
given time, another 4 ha lying fallow. Each farm is usually divided into 4
or 5 separate plots. The most important food crop is cassava, grown both for
consumption and sale, and the heavy demands of this crop upon soil fertility
in the more densely populated areas leads to shortening of the fallow period.
Yam and plantain are also widely grown and marketed, and smaller quantities
of cocoyam and maize are produced, mostly for family consumption. Oil palms
1/   The "Family" in this context means the extended family or kindred unit
and may include several generations.



- 8 -
are widespread, mostly in wild groves but occasionally on smallholdings but
maintenance standards are low. Coconut palms are not common, despite the
apparent suitability of soil and climatic conditions.
Communications
3.10      Communications to and within the project area are exclusively by
road. The area contains 202 km of existing asphalt roads and about 1000 km
of earth roads and tracks. Main roads in the north, west and south are
satisfactory, but the east is served only by earth tracks. However, two
asphalt roads to the area are planned for construction before 1980.
3.11      The road density is high (0.50 km/km ) but earth tracks (80% of the
network) are difficult if not impossible to pass in the rainy season. Further-
more, the area east of the Otamiri and Ogochia rivers is isolated from the rest
of the project area as there are no motorable bridges across the rivers. A
temporary bridge over the Otamiri is scheduled for construction shortly by the
State Government, but construction of the Ogochia crossing has not yet been
scheduled.
IV. THE PROJECT
A.   General Description
4.01      The primary objective of the project would be to assist in partly
filling the shortfall in domestic palm oil requirements, estimated at some
190,000 tons per annum by 1985 (see para. 2.07). This would be achieved by
establishing 20,000 hectares of high yielding oil palm on a nucleus estate and
associated smallholdings, which would produce 47,250 tons of palm oil at full
development in 1990. In addition, the project includes the improvement or
reconstruction of about 400 km of earth roads serving the smallholder community,
feasibility studies for a second phase oil palm project and a coconut project,
and a coconut seed garden. The project would be implemented over a six and a
half year period, 1978-1984.
4.02      Planting would be carried out over the period 1978 through 1982 as
Phase I of a 40,000 ha program during the period 1977 through 1986.
Planting Program
1977   1978   1979   1980   1981   1982   Total
Nucleus Estate       -       500  2,200  3,300  4,000    -       10,000
Smallholders         -      200  1,000  2,200  3,300  3,300   10,000
Total           -      700  3,200  5,500  7,300  3,300   20,000



- 9 -
4.03      The Project, which in concept and design is largely as proposed in
the SOCFINCO Ltd. feasibility studies, includes four main components:
(i) Establishing a Nucleus Estate
(a) planting 10,000 ha by the State-owned company
RISONPALM Ltd, using a management agency for
implementation;
(b) constructing and operating one palm oil mill
to process estate and smallholders production;
and
(c) establishing a fruit collection system to be
operated by RISONPALM for estate and smallholder
production.
(ii) New Planting/Replanting Smallholdings
(a) establishing a Smallholder Management Unit (SMU)
within the Rivers State Ministry of Agriculture
and Natural Resources to implement the smallholder
planting program (10,000 ha);
(b) operating a Grant/Credit scheme for participating
smallholders;
(c) training staff and smallholders at selected insti-
tutions and through in-service courses; and
(d) improving about 400 km of earth roads to facilitate
all-weather distribution of inputs and collections
of fruit bunches from smallholders.
(iii) Coconut ProJect Identification and Preparation Study and
Establishing a Coconut Seed Garden of 120 ha over the
period 1978 through 1982.
(iv) Feasibility Study for Phase II Oil Palm Project
B.   Detailed Features
(i) Nucleus Estate
4.04      The estate component would form part of the oil palm development
of RISONPALM Ltd. - a recently established state-owned limited liability
company. Development of the estate would be carried out under a management
contract with an internationally recognized firm acceptable to the Bank.
For details see Chapter VI, paras. 6.07 and 6.08.



- 10 -
4.05      Planting Program.  RISONPALM would develop 10,000 ha east of Elele
oil palm estate, between Umu Dioga in the west and Ozuzu in the east (see
Map IBRD 12590). Planting would be carried out from 1978 through 1981 as
Phase I of a 20,000 ha nucleus estate program through 1985/86. To attract
a resident estate labor force for maintenance and harvesting 400 houses would
be built, each with an 0.2 ha vegetable plot. As labor is expected to be
in short supply it will not be possible to clear the land manually. For
this reason project costs include heavy equipment to enable land clearing
to be carried out on force account by the estate. The Nigerian Institute
for Oil Palm Research (NIFOR) is expected to supply Grade I wilt-tolerant
seeds to the project. During negotiations assurances were obtained that
Rivers State would obtain confirmation from NIFOR not later than September 1
each year, that the Institute would supply the required seeds for the follow-
ing season, failing which the project would be free to order from other
available sources including sources outside Nigeria.
4.06      Palm Oil Mills.  Fruit production, which would not start until 1982,
would initially be processed at the existing Elele plantation mill. The
20,000 ha planting program under the project would require, at full production,
two palm oil mills. One of the mills would be financed under the project and
construction would start in 1980. Construction of the second oil mill would
start in 1983. One mill would be located in the south of N.W. Estate and the
other east of the N.E. Estate, near the Otamiri River. The first mill would
reach a capacity of 40 tons/hour by 1985 and the second mill 40 tons/hour by
1989. Phasing of the mills would be as follows:
1982   1983   1984   1985   1986   1987   1988    1989
Required Capacity
(tons ffb/hour)      2.8    7.3   18.7   36.0   47.7   56.1   60.2    62.5
Installed Capacity
(tons ffb/hour)
Mill 1             -      10     20      40     40      40     40       40
Mill 2             -       -      -       -     10      20     30       40
Total           -     10      20     40      50      60     70       80
From 1990 onwards capacity required by Phase I plantings would be at least
65 tons/hour, leaving a small reserve.  During negotiations assurances were
obtained that early in 1979 RISONPALM would engage consultants satisfactory to
the State and the Bank to prepare design specifications and tender procedures
for the first palm oil mill; the design specifications and tender procedures
would have to be satisfactory to the Bank. Invitations to tender would be
issued at least three years before the mills are scheduled to start operating;
tenders for the first mill would be issued at the end of 1979 and for the
second mill at the end of 1982. Contractors would be required to provide
training for Nigerian technical staff in Europe at plants producing mill



- 11 -
equipment and in Nigeria during mill erection and commissioning. Mill capac-
ity must meet estate and smallholder production levels and during negotiations
it was agreed that it would be a condition of disbursement for the mill that
the Bank had received satisfactory confirmation from SMU and the Monitoring
and Evaluation Unit (MEU) of the Federal Department of Agriculture that the
mill could process all oil palm fruit produced by the nucleus estate and
smallholders.
4.07      SMU and RISONPALMI shall agree on an acceptable percentage of esti-
mated smallholder production to be delivered to the mills for processing.
Assurance to this effect was obtained during negotiations.
4.08      Fruit Collection System.  At full maturity annual FFB production
would be 125,000 tons on the nucleus estates and 100,000 tons on the small-
holdings, with 800 tons and 600 tons respectively being collected daily in
the peak months. The maximum distance smallholders would be required to carry
bunches would be 600 m to the roadside. The bunches would be heaped in nets
distributed to farmers by the project and hoisted by cranes mounted on the
collecting vehicles. At collection points, bunches would be weighed and the
farmer would be given a receipt for the amount and quantity of fruit delivered.
Collection would be organized to take place over a week, on the same day at
the collecting point; it would be identical in the nucleus estates with the
exception that nets would be distributed to harvesters on the day of collection
(details are at Annex 6). The organization of the fruit collection system
would be the responsibility of the estate and oil mills management (para. 6.10).
(ii) Smallholders
4.09      The Smallholders Management Unit (SMU) would be responsible to the
Rivers State Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources (MAINR) for the
smallholder component. SMU would have a high degree of financial and admin-
istrative autonomy and would be responsible for appraising smallholder appli-
cations and supervising the technical and financial services involved in
the field establishment and post-maturity stages. The SMU planting program
of 10,000 ha would be carried out from 1978 through 1982. Details of SMU's
structure and functions are given in paras. 6.02, 6.03, 6.04, 6.05 and
Annex 10.
4.10      Grant and Credit Scheme.  In the early 1960's an oil palm re-
habilitation scheme was introduced to the then Eastern Region (now Imo State,
Rivers State and Cross River State) under which the problem of providing
producers with an incentive to replant was overcome by providing smallholders
with grants-now an integral part of FMG's agricultural development policy -
consisting of cash payments, planting materials and other farm inputs. In
1975, three Bank-financed oil palm projects were approved under which small-
holders are provided with grants in kind and credit in cash that, combined
with fair producer prices, are expected to provide farmers with sufficient
incentive to plant palms that take nearly four years to come into bearing and
produce revenue. Smallholder participation in these projects, which are still
in the establishment phase, has been good. Under the proposed project small-
holders' returns (para 7.08) justify applying the same grant/credit incentive.



- 12 -
4.11      The project would provide grants and credits to smallholders planting
or replanting a minimum of 1 ha to a maximum of 10 ha. One ha is the smallest
unit that is considered practical to administer under the project, while at
maturity more than 10 ha would require the average farm family to hire labor
for its oil palm and other food crop cultivation. In order to strengthen the
local cooperative movement to the stage where it could eventually assume many
of the functions of SMU, participants would be expected to be members of
registered or officially recognized cooperatives and would be required to sign
grant/credit agreements with SMU and their cooperatives (para 6.06). During
negotiations assurances were obtained that these arrangements would be employed
and that the terms of the agreements would be acceptable to the Bank.
4.12      The total costs of clearing, planting and maintaining the palms for
four years until they come into production are estimated in constant 1976
terms at about N672 (US$1,035) per hectare. SMU would distribute (a) as grant
and in kind, palm seedlings, cover crop seeds, wire netting, fertilizers and
chemicals to a value of N260 (US$400) per hectare and (b) as credit, in cash,
N300 (US$462) for payment of labor. The credit would bear interest at 9.5%
for 13 years with 7 years grace during which interest would be capitalized.
Each smallholder would contribute about N110 (US$169) per ha in the form of
labor and tools. Monthly repayments would be calculated on a per hectare
basis and deducted from each smallholders ffb proceeds. In constant 1976
terms monthly repayments by the smallholder would amount to N9.0O (US$13.86)
per ha, and the cooperative would repay to SMU N8.00 (US$12.32) equivalent to
an annual interest of 7.5% (see para. 6.06). Cash payments during the pre-
harvesting period would only be made after physical inspection to ensure that
technical standards laid down by SMU had been followed. The loan agreement
between SMU, the cooperative and the smallholder would include an undertaking
by the smallholder to sell his ffb to the nucleus estate mills. Details of
the grant/credit scheme are in Annex 5.
4.13      Training.  Senior and middle level SMU and Nucleus Estate staff
would receive training at the Management Training Institute (ARMTI) to be
established by the Federal Ministry of Agriculture and Rural Development.
Technical training would be provided for Senior Officers, Nursery Staff, Field
Staff and Smallholders of the SMU. There are shortages of trained and expe-
rienced Nigerian technical staff at many of the levels required by this
project, and it is therefore clear that the project would have to do a lot of
training and upgrading of staff itself. Agricultural Officers (AO) or above
would be given 3-6 months training in planting techniques and management on
large successful oil palm development schemes or commercial farms. Agricul-
tural Assistants (AA) supervising nurseries would receive twelve months
special training in nursery techniques from germinated seed state to field
planting.  Other Agricultural Assistants would be given a 2-3 months intensive
course in modern planting techniques at the Nigerian Institute for Oil Palm
Research (NIFOR) followed by a similar period of practical training in the
supervision of field operations on existing estates. In addition, all SMU



- 13 -
field staff would be trained in smallholder appraisal and grant/credit super-
vision procedures. Staff would also be trained elsewhere in West Africa and
for some senior officers possibly in the Far East for which funds are provided.
Smallholder training would be developed on a group activity basis, with some
courses of I to 2 day duration, and would include: (a) outlining application/
appraisal procedures and loan agreement conditions, (b) demonstrating plant-
ing, maintenance and harvesting practices. A training schedule and cost
estimates are given in Annex 8 table 17.
4.14      Road Improvement.  No improvement of paved roads is required in
the project area but earth roads require upgrading. The proposed fruit
collection road network is 592 km (164 km of existing and 78 km of planned
asphalt roads, and 350 km of earth roads). In addition, a further 50 km
of earth roads are required to link the smallholders' area with the oil
mills. Construction costs of asphalt roads (estimated at N 9.4 million)
are not included in the project as they form part of the State and Federal
development plans 1976-1980. However, improvement of 400 km of earth roads
by a Roads Division in SMU is included in project costs. A temporary bridge
over the Ogochia river is also included; this must be constructed to serve
the project planting program, as the timing for construction of a permanent
bridge (which is part of the State Development Plan) is uncertain. Total
cost of the road component is estimated at N 1.3 million, including estab-
lishing and operating an equipment servicing workshop. Maintenance costs
following the 4 year construction period are estimated at N 80,000 per annum.
In line with Government policy the Local Government Authorities (LGA's) would
assume this responsibility (for details see Annex 4).
(iii) Coconut Project Identification and Preparation Study and
Establishment of Coconut Seed Garden
4.15      Both the Federal and State Governments are interested in small-
holder and estate coconut development for which many areas of Southern
Nigeria are well suited. Yields of 4 and 4.5 tons of copra per ha can be
expected from smallholdings and estates respectively, using improved hybrid
dwarf x tall material. However, any coconut development would be contingent
upon a local supply of high yielding material.
4.16      To determine whether coconut development is feasible, the project
includes about 12 man-months of consultants costing US$134,000 for coconut
project identification and preparation in 1978. The study would: (a) assess
prospects for developing coconuts in the tree crop belt of Nigeria, including
assessment of Kaincope disease, (b) prepare proposals for smallholder and
estate coconut development. If the study demonstrates that coconut development
would be viable, establishment of a coconut seed garden would be scheduled for
initial planting in 1979. It is proposed that a 120 ha seed garden of hybrid
dwarf x tall coconuts would be established during the period 1979-1982. The
seed garden would comprise 100 ha of "mother trees" (yellow and red dwarfs)
and 20 ha of "pollinators" (selected West African and Pacific Talls), the
selected seeds being imported from Ivory Coast, Benin and the Far East.
During negotiations assurances were obtained that the consultants selected



- 14 -
for the coconut project identification and preparation study would be accept-
able to the Bank and that the results of the study would be submitted to the
Bank for comment. It was agreed during negotiations that it would be a con-
dition of disbursement against the coconut seed garden that the coconut de-
velopment proposals are satisfactory to the Bank. (Details are given in
Annex 7).
(iv) Feasibility Study of Phase II Oil Palm Project
4.17      The project includes six man-months of consultants costing US$67,000
to prepare a Phase II project (para. 4.03) for implementation starting in 1983,
emphasizing expansion of smallholder oil palm plantings and completion of the
oil palm mills started under this project (para. 4.06). During negotiations
assurances were obtained that the terms of reference for the study and the
consultants chosen to undertake it would be acceptable to the Bank.
V. COST ESTIMATES AND FINANCIAL ARRANGEMENTS

cluding start-up costs in 1977/1978, are estimated at N54.0 million (US$83.0
million). If taxes and duties are excluded costs would be N52.7 million
(US$81.2 million). The foreign exchange component would be N19.4 million
(US$30.0 million) or 36% of total project costs.

5.03      Cost estimates are based on prices obtained during appraisal,
updated to reflect mid-1977 prices, and include a 5% physical contingency.
Price contingencies are calculated on base line costs plus physical contin-
gency and allow for compounded increases in foreign exchange costs of: (a)
vehicles, equipment, utilities and furniture and farm inputs of 4.5%, in the
second half of 1977, 7% in 1978, 6.5% in 1979 and 6% thereafter; (b) housing,
buildings, oil mill, construction of roads and consultant services of 4.5%
in July-December 1977, 8% in 1978, 7.5% in 1979 and 7% thereafter; and (c)
salaries and operating costs of 3.5% in late 1977 and 7% thereafter. A price
contingency of 20% compounded annually has been applied for all local costs,
other than salaries and hired labor, to account for internal inflation. 1/
Contingencies are equivalent to 34% of total costs.

5.05      The Bank loan of US$30.0 million (36% of project costs) would be
equivalent to the foreign exchange costs, including US$2.2 million in 1977/
1978, the start-up year. It would finance about 40% of the nucleus estate
component or N 10.1 million (US$15.5 million), 45% of the coconut seed garden
or N 0.4 million (US$0.6 million) and 25% of Smallholder Management Unit or
N2.3 million (US$3.6 million), while N 6.6 million (US$10.1 million) would be
reserved for unallocated expenditures.
5.06      The Bank loan would be made to FMG for 20 years, including five
years grace on repayment of principal and onlent to Rivers State Government on
the same terms. FMG would provide an allocation equivalent to 25% of project
cost amounting to N 13.3 million (US$20.5 million). Assurances to these
effects were obtained during negotiations. It was agreed during negotia-
tions that the execution of a subsidiary loan agreement between FMG and the
State Government, which would cover both onlending of the Bank loan and the
FMG allocation duly authorized or ratified by all necessary corporate and
Governmental action, would be a condition of effectiveness.  The Rivers State
Government would finance 37% of project cost or N 20.1 million (US$30.9 mil-
lion) from the State budget and participating smallholders 2% or N 1.2 mil-
lion (US$1.8 million) in the form of labor and tools.
5.07      In turn, a portion of the Bank loan amounting to N 10.1 million
(US$15.5 million), plus N 8.0 million (US$12.3 million) of the total State
contribution and N 6.1 million (US$9.4 million) of the FMG total contribution
would be onlent to (or as necessary invested as equity in) RISONPALM Ltd.,
by the State Government to cover part of the nucleus estate cost. The loan
amounting to N 50.2 million (US$77.3 million) would be at 9% per annum for 25
years including a grace period of 10 years.  Due to the RISONPALM cash flow
position in the early years an extended grace period is required. In addition,
and in any case, there would be an equity contribution to RISONPALM Ltd. by
the State Government of at least N 4.2 million (US$6.5 million). Assurances
to these effects were obtained during negotiations. It was agreed during
negotiations that the execution of a subsidiary loan and equity subscription
agreement between Rivers State Government and RISONPALM Ltd. duly authorized
or ratified by all necessary corporate or governmental action would be a
condition of effectiveness. The proportion of the Bank loan, and FMG and
State Government contributions to total nucleus estate project costs would be
40%, 25% and 30% respectively.
5.08      The financing arrangements detailed in paras. 5.05 through 5.07
cover the funds needed for the six and a half year development period plus
start-up costs in 1977/1978 totalling N 4.0 million (US$6.2 million). The
start-up costs include foreign exchange costs of N 1.4 million (US$2.2 mil-
lion) which are included in the Bank loan, N 1.0 million (US$1.5 million)
provided by the Federal Government, N 1.6 million (US$2.5 million) provided
by the Rivers State Government.
5.09      To avoid delays in project implementation, heavy equipment must
be purchased to clear land for planting in 1978 and international staff
must be recruited as soon as possible after negotiations. It was agreed



- 18 -
during negotiations that retroactive financing of up to US$800,000 be made
available for the purchase of heavy equipment and US$450,000 for recruitment
of international staff, for eligible expenditures incurred after January 1,
1977.
C.   Procurement
5.10      Vehicles, tractors, fertilizers, chemicals, road-making equip-
ment, other equipment and farm inputs valued at about N 10.3 million (US$15.8
million) net of contingencies would be procured through international com-
petitive bidding in accordance with Bank guidelines for contracts over
US$75,000, and through competitive bidding and in accordance with local
procedures satisfactory to the Bank for contracts less than US$75,000. For
contracts under US$10,000, direct competitive shopping would be employed.
Domestically manufactured goods would be allowed a 15% preference or the
applicable import duty, whichever is lower. Experience has shown that con-
tracts for houses, stores and buildings, which are valued at N 6.0 million
(US$9.2 million), are not attractive to foreign contractors due to dispersed
locations, small size of contracts and competitiveness of local contractors;
such contracts would be awarded on the basis of local competitive bidding.
Construction of roads would be undertaken by the project on force account
in view of the absence of local contractors working on earth road construc-
tion. A large part of project cost would be salaries, operating expenses,
training costs and field establishment costs valued at N 16.9 million (US$26.0
million) which are unsuitable for competitive bidding. The services of
consultants and expatriate staff valued at N 2.3 million (US$3.5 million)
would be obtained in accordance with procedures acceptable to the Bank.
An amount of N 18.5 million (US$28.5 million) would cover contingencies.
5.11      During negotiations assurances were obtained that the following
supplemental procurement procedures shall apply in the procurement of fer-
tilizer by the Federal Fertilizer Unit (FFU) of the Federal Ministry of
Agriculture and Rural Development on behalf of RISONPALM and SMU:
(i) RISONPALM and SMU shall furnish FFU, no later than 16
months next preceeding April 1st of the year of util-
ization, with details of the quantities, specifications
and packaging of fertilizer required, and details of
the timing and delivery points for the fertilizer;
(ii) FFU shall prepare tender documents for the said fertil-
izer in accordance with procedures consistent with
those set forth in the Bank's Guidelines referred to
in Schedule 1 to the Project Agreement for the Project
on the basis of international competitive bidding
(ICB);
(iii) after review by RISONPALM and SMU, FFU shall issue
tenders;



- 19 -
(iv) FFU shall review and evaluate returned bids, and after
review with RISONPALM, SMU and the Bank award, before
April 1st of the year preceeding utilization, contracts
following ICB procedures; and
(v) after receipt of the fertilizer at RISONPALM and SMU
stores, RISONPALM and SMU shall on the basis of satis-
factory shipping documents provided by FFU, request
the Bank to reimburse the foreign exchange cost of the
fertilizer to FFU.
(vi) In the event that the contracts for fertilizer have not
been signed by April 1st of the year preceeding utiliza-
tion, then management of both RISONPALM and SMU shall
have the right to procure their fertilizer requirements
directly under ICB procedures. However, in view of time
constraints fertilizer requirements for 1979 shall be
ordered for delivery by FFU by the end of January 1979.
D.   Disbursements
5.12      The Bank loan would be disbursed during 1978-1984 as follows:
Category 1 - Nucleus Estate:
(a) 100% of foreign exchange costs of field establishment,
(excluding labor, locally procured seeds and cover
crops and land acquisition) housing, civil works,
offices, stores, and other buildings or (if locally
procured) 35% of these expenditures, totalling US$8.1
million;
(b) 100% of foreign exchange costs of vehicles and equipment
international staff salaries, consultants, feasibility
study, training, management fee and operating costs or (if
locally procured) 75% of these costs, totalling US$12.8
million.
Category 2 - Coconut Seed Garden:
100% of the foreign exchange costs of the coconut seed
garden or (if locally procured) 45% of these expenditures,
totalling US$0.8 million.
Category 3 - Smallholder Management Unit
(a) 100% of foreign exchange costs of field establishment
(excluding labor, locally procured seeds and cover crops)
and housing or (if locally procured) 50% of these costs,
totalling US$1.9 million;



- 20 -
(b) 100% of foreign exch,ange costs of road construction (ex-
cluding local staff salaries), staff training, interna-
tional staff salaries, vehicles and equipment, consultants
and operating costs or (if locally procured) 30% of these
expenditures, totalling US$3.7 million.
Category 4 - An unallocated amount of US$2.7 million.
5.13      Disbursements would be fully documented.  Those against field
establishment and operating costs in Categories 1 (a and b), 2 and 3 (a and b)
would be against certificates of expenditure to be retained by the nucleus
estate or SMU for review by supervision missions and the auditors.
E.   Budgets, Accounts and Audits
5.14      Annual budgets would be prepared by the nucleus estate for approval
by the Board of RISONPALM Ltd. and by SMU for approval by its Steering Com-
mittee (see para. 6.02). These budgets as approved shall be furnished to RSG
and FMG for their review not later than five months before the commencement
of each fiscal year. The nucleus estate and SMU would prepare quarterly
cash flow statements which would be submitted to the RISONPALM Board and
SMU Steering Committee respectively and would operate on the basis of these
statements unless informed otherwise. The nucleus estate and SMU would
prepare for prompt submission to the Bank, quarterly and annual progress
reports including summaries of expenditures and use of funds. During negotia-
tions assurances were obtained that the above procedures would be followed.
5.15      On the basis of the approved annual budgets, *the State and Federal
Governments would make budgetary allocations and, thereafter, would make
available to the nucleus estate and SMU all necessary funds quarterly and
in advance. Separate bank accounts for the nucleus estate and SMU would be
opened and adequately funded, and this would be a condition of effectiveness.
5.16      The nucleus estate and SMU would keep records, in accordance
with sound accounting practices, to reflect their operations and financial
positions and would have these audited by a firm of independent external
auditors acceptable to the Bank; the audited accounts and the auditor's
report would be submitted to the Bank within four months of the end of the
fiscal year. Assurances to this effect were obtained during negotiations.
VI. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
Organization and Management
6.01      Project implementation would involve:  the Smallholder Management
Unit (SMU), RISONPALM Ltd, and the Federal Department of Agriculture's Moni-
toring and Evaluation Unit (MEU).



- 21 -
Smallholder Management Unit (SMU)
6.02      General.  SMU would be responsible for the smallholder program.  It
would have a high degree of financial and administrative autonomy as a compo-
nent of the Rivers State Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources (MANR).
During negotiations it was agreed that the publication in the Gazette, notices
establishing SMU and the Steering Committee in a form satisfactory to the Bank
and that SMIJ's management positions have been filled would be conditions of
effectiveness.  The Steering Committee, chaired by the Permanent Secretary of
MANR and comprised of Government agency representatives, would determine SMU
policy, approve work programs and budgets and review quarterly reports.
Details of the SMUJ structure and operational procedures are in Annex 10. A
mechanized accounting system would be set up for SMU by consultants whose
qualifications, experience, terms and conditions of employment (including
terms of reference) shall be satisfactory to the Bank. During negotiations
assurances to this effect were obtained.
6.03      Management Structure and Staffing.  SMU headquarters would be
at Ahoada with regional units at Ahoada, Isiokpo and Okehi. SMU would be
staffed primarily with existing MANR personnel. At headquarters, senior
management would comprise a General Manager, Controller of Field Operations,
Controller of Finance, and Roads Engineer. These posts would be filled by
staff with qualifications, experience and terms and conditions of service
satisfactory to the Bank. During negotiations assurance to this effect was
obtained. If suitably qualified Nigerians are not available, the posts would
be filled initially through international recruiting. The Managers of the
three regional units would be equivalent to experienced senior agricultural
officers.
6.04      Operational Procedures.  Notices calling for applications from
smallholders and giving details of the scheme would be publicized through
local newspapers, radio and public administration offices. To make efficient
use of field staff, particularly during planting when the heaviest intensity
of farm visits is required, a zonal system would be used. Thus, in any given
year, smallholder applications would be restricted to specific zones. Any
shortfall could be met by having an adjacent reserve zone for which applica-
tions could be requested but only accepted if required. Applications would
be called in January and closed in March preceding the planting year. SMU
would inspect applicant's smallholdings to ascertain suitability and eligi-
bility and, in consultation with the cooperatives, the farmer's credit status.
Applications would be approved by a Planting Authorization Committee (PAC)
composed of SMU General Manager as Chairman, Controller of Field Operations,
Controller of Finance and a cooperative societies representative.
6.05      SMU would provide technical supervision and advice and ensure that:
(i) if Eupatorium covered more than 30% of the area to be planted, then SMU
shall assist the smallholder in clearing this weed by providing spraying
facilities the cost of which would be charged to the smallholder's credit
account; (ii) old palm trees were felled before new planting/replanting;
and (iii) only controlled intercropping during the first two years would



- 22 -
be permitted in the new planting of palms. Maintenance of suitable hus-
bandry standards including those above would be conditions of smallholder
participation. During negotiations assurances to these effects were obtained.
6.06      Purchase of Smallholder ffb.  The mill would provide a schedule
of ffb purchases and amounts due to smallholders, and credit SMU who, after
deducting loan repayments due to the State Government, would pay the balance
to each cooperative. The cooperative, after deducting the interest spread,
would distribute the balance to its members (see para. 4.12). The coopera-
tives would guarantee the repayment of the loans by their members.
RISONPALM Ltd.
6.07      RISONPALM Ltd. is a limited liability company formed on November 24,
1975 in accordance with the Companies Decree of 1968, and is responsible for
development of the oil palm estates formerly operated by Rivers State Agricul-
tural Production and Marketing Corporation (APMC). The members of the Board
of RISONPALM were appointed on April 4, 1977 by the Rivers State Executive
Council and include representatives from the Ministries of Agriculture,
Finance, and Trade and Economic Development (Details are in Annex 10).
6.08      Management of the Nucleus Estate Unit (NEU) would be provided by
management consultants, under a management agreement with terms and conditions
acceptable to the Bank. Senior management staff would initially comprise a
General Manager, Financial Controller, Two Field Managers and two Mechanical
Engineers all of whom would be appointed with qualifications, experience and
terms and conditions of service satisfactory to the Bank. During negotiations
assurances to these effects were obtained. It was agreed during negotiations
that the execution of this Management Agreement and the appointment of these
key staff would be conditions of effectiveness.
6.09      Responsibility for construction and operation of the project oil
mill would be included in the Management Agreement (see para. 6.08). Senior
management for the mill would comprise a Mill Manager, a Processing Engineer
and subsequently a Maintenance Engineer. Staff recruited would be appointed
with qualifications, experience and terms and conditions of service acceptable
to the Bank, and where suitably qualified Nigerians were not available the
posts would initially be filled through international recruitment. The
management agreement would specify that Nigerian personnel should be provided
with training and on-the-job managerial experience to enable them to fill
senior managerial and technical positions at an early stage.
6.10      Management of NEU and the mill would be responsible for establish-
ment and operation of the fruit collection system for both the estate and
the smallholders (see para. 4.08).
Monitoring and Evaluation Unit (MEU)
6.11      The Monitoring and Evaluation Unit of the Federal Department of
Agriculture was established in Benin City, Bendel State in 1975 under Loan
1183-UNI. Its role is to monitor financial and technical performance of



- 23 -
projects to evaluate their economic and social impact, and to assist in the
planning of new projects. MEU would be involved in monitoring and evaluating
the proposed project but no further financing is required.
6.12      The progress of MEU to date has been mixed.  After initial recruit-
ment delays, the international complement of staff will soon be completed.
Good progress has been recorded on the technical monitoring aspect, and a
start is now being made on the economic evaluation. The demands of admin-
istration have impeded the work of financial monitoring. However, the main
weakness of the unit so far has been the difficulty in recruiting Nigerian
counterpart staff, and until this problem can be overcome the future of the
unit cannot be assured.
VII. PRODUCTION, MARKETING, FARMER BENEFITS,
FINANCIAL IMPLICATIONS TO GOVERNMENT
Yields and Production
7.01      Nucleus estate and smallholder oil palms would begin yielding in
the fourth year after planting. At full maturity the estates are expected
to yield 12.5 tons of fresh fruit bunches (ffb) per ha but smallholder yields
are estimated at 10 tons per ha, reflecting lower standards of maintenance.
Oil extraction rates are assumed to rise from 18% in the first harvesting
year to 21% at full maturity in the fourth year; kernels are estimated
at 4.5% of ffb. These estimates are in line with those experienced with
equivalent planting material under similar ecological conditions in West
Africa.
7.02      At full maturity in 1990 the project would produce annually about
47,250 tons of palm oil and 10,126 tons of kernels. The nucleus estates
would produce about 56% of total output and project smallholders the balance
(see Annex 3).
Market and Prices
7.03      In 1975, palm oil production in Nigeria was estimated at 500,000 tons
of which all but 35,000 tons was consumed domestically. This is in marked
contrast to the pre-civil war period when exports were considerable; for
example, in 1965 production was 574,000 tons and exports were 152,000 tons.
Annex I discusses likely trends in domestic supply and demand. Some oil
palm areas were destroyed or damaged during the civil war, but new plantings
are now coming into bearing, and production in 1985 is estimated at 640,000
tons per annum. Demand forecasts have drawn on a Bank study on the relation-
ship between income levels and consumption of fats and oils. Assuming a
population growth rate of 2.5% annually and a GNP growth rate of 6.4% per
annum to 1980 and 4.0% thereafter, and allowing for some substitution for
palm oil, total domestic demand for palm oil in 1985 is projected at approxi-
mately 800,000 tons. This indicates that, without the proposed project there
will be a supply deficit of up to 200,000 tons of palm oil per annum by 1985.



- 24 -
7.04      Commodity Boards are authorized to purchase all palm oil and kernels
offered for sale. Under these arrangements the Boards have, in the past,
purchased oil surplus to domestic requirements and exported it through the
Nigerian Produce Mark-ting Company (NPMC). Oil available for export in Rivers
State has declined rapidly in recent years and is now nonexistent, but the
domestic market for palm kernels is limited and the bulk continues to be
exported. The Marketing Boards, which largely functioned in the past as a
taxation mechanism rather than a price stabilization agency, jealously guarded
their rights to purchase palm oil surplus to domestic needs, and in particular
considered estate production to be for export. In recent years, however,
estates have sold oil domest:ically although the Boards retain their purchasing
authority. Given this situation, assurance was obtained during negotiations
that RISONPALM Ltd. would be permitted to sell project produced palm oil at
the most favorable market va:Lue. This assurance was obtained in the case of
the previous Bank assisted Oil Palm projects.
7.05      Domestic palm oil prices have risen considerably in recent years,
and now exceed world market 'Levels in the absence of imports. There is
considerable geographical and seasonal variation, but in Rivers State, 1976
retail prices for 4 gallon tins varied between N 600 and N840 per metric ton
(US$900 to US$1,430 at current exchange rates). Prices for a 0.65 liter
bottle were often higher but showed greater variations. The comparable Market-
ing Board purchase price was N 295 per ton for SPO (Special Grade Palm Oil) and
N 265 for TPO (Technical Grade Palm Oil) and producers who can do so naturally
prefer to sell in the free market. If, as expected, Nigeria becomes a major
importer of palm oil it is questionable whether domestic prices will remain so
much higher than international prices. For project benefits, therefore, the
Bank's forecast of world market prices has been used as a basis (see Annex 15).
7.06      Marketing Boards have in recent years purchased 90% of palm kernel
production. Until 1973 the Boards fixed purchase prices at a level substan-
tially below export prices. Subsequently an attempt was made to set fairer
producer prices. However, in 1975-76 world kernel prices more than halved from
1974 levels, leaving Marketing Board producer prices well above world prices.
In calculating project benefits kernel producer prices have been based on the
Bank's projected 1985 world market price (see Annex 15).
Farmer Benefits
7.07      Most project farm families would be able to plant and maintain
2 ha of oil palm without affecting present farming activities. Assuming
development of the 2 ha at one time, production would begin in the fourth
year from planting and full bearing would be reached in the eighth year.
Over the four-year development period farmers would receive a gross return
from the cash element of the grant/credit equal to about N 1.70 per manday
worked; the current unofficial farm wage rate is about N 2.00. A farm budget
is given in Annex 12.
7.08      Assuming a farmgate price of N 40.0 per ton of ffb, the gross
return (in constant 1976 terms) from 2 ha would start at N 254.0 in the
first year of production (year 4) rising to N 578.0 during the farmer's



- 25 -
repayment period (Years 7-12) and thereafter would average about N 814.0.
These returns compare favorably with those obtainable from alternative
tree or food crops even where improved management and planting material
are used. A 2-ha planting would require about 60 mandays a year for
maintenance and harvesting, and gross returns per manday would rise rapidly
from N 3.9 in the year of first production (Year 4) to an average of N 8.6
during Years 7-12 of loan repayment and N 12.9 thereafter.
7.09      Official data on smallholder farm incomes are sketchy.  However,
a recent unofficial socio-economic survey of the project area conciuded
that "60% of families have an annual income of between N600 and N1,600".
It may therefore be concluded that, for a relatively low labor input, a
holding of 1 or 2 hectares of improved oil palm could make a substantial
contribution to family incomes.
7.10      The farm budget calculations are deliberately conservative using
a ffb purchase price of N40.0 per ton, which is compatible with long term
international price forecasts (in real terms). If oil prices on the Nigerian
market remained at or close to recent levels, farm incomes could exceed the
forecast subject to increased costs, particularly labor. The actual purchase
price paid by RISONPALM to smallholders would be determined periodically by
a Price Policy Committee (PPC) which shall be established by January 1, 1982
in a form satisfactory to the Bank. It shall be responsible for the estab-
lishment, in accordance with principles acceptable to the Bank of a formula
for calculating the price of fresh fruit bunches of oil palm purchased by
RISONPALM from smallholders participating in the project. During negotiations
assurances to these effects were obtained. The formula proposed is discussed
in detail in Annex 11, and is in line with the arrangements agreed for the
previous Bank-assisted oil palm projects. The formula is designed to give the
smallholders a price that fairly reflects the revenues from oil sales at
prevailing market prices, while making provision for the mills to cover their
costs including depreciation, and earn a reasonable return on capital.
Financial Returns to RISONPALM Ltd.
7.11      RISONPALM Ltd. is a wholly owned State Government company and cash
deficits and surpluses ought to be considered in the light of the State Govern-
ment cash flow. The projected cash flow for RISONPALM Ltd. in 1976 constant
prices is in Annex 13. The cash flow shows that there would be annual cash
surpluses from 1982 through 1984; deficits would result thereafter through
1989, largely as a result of debt service repayment to State Government. The
peak annual deficit during this period would be N 2.3 million (US$3.5 million)
which would have to be met from commercial bank overdraft facilities or by the
State Government. From 2002 onwards, when State Government debt services are
completed annual surpluses would be N 9.2 million (US$14.2 million). These
surpluses would be available to meet increased smallholder producer prices,
for investment in further oil palm development, and for distribution to State
Government.



- 26 -
Financial Implication for State Government
7.12      The State Government cash flow is at Annex 14.  The cash flow
incorporates the IBRD loan and the Federal Goverment grant but it does
not take account of surpluses arising in RISONPALM Ltd. which is a wholly
owned State company (see para 7.11). From 1977 through 1987 the cumulative
deficit would rise from N 1.0 million (US$1.5 million) to N 44.0 million
(US$57.8 million). From 1994 through 2001 annual surpluses would accrue, by
which time the cumulative deficit would have been converted to a cumulative
surplus of N 62.2 million (US$95.8 million). Thus while there would be a
substantial burden on the State Government budget until full production is
reached, thereafter substantial surpluses would accrue. On this basis ex-
ternal financing of Phase II of the project from 1983 through 1986 is
warranted.
VIII. BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
8.01      The principal direct benefits from the project would be additional
annual production of 47,250 tons of palm oil and 10,126 tons of kernels at
full maturity in 1990. In world prices (constant 1976 terms) this would
have a value of US$23 million. The palm oil would substitute for imports,
while kernels would be exported thus making a significant contribution to
the Nigerian balance of payments.
8.02      The economic rate of return is estimated at 15.2%.  Principal
assumptions are at Annex 15, including the sensitivity analysis. A 10%
reduction in benefit together with a 10% increase in costs reduces the
rate of return to 12.2%. The rate of return is comparable to similar
oil palm projects in Nigeria and elsewhere in West Africa.
8.03      At full maturity, there would be about 2,000 full time employment
opportunities created on the estate, in mills and with SMU. In addition,
extra employment and incomes would be generated and improved within the
smallholder sector. About 5,000 families (35,000 people) would benefit
directly from the smallholder component.
8.04      The project would involve training a considerable number of local
personnel in agricultural extension and management techniques and administra-
tion of agricultural credit schemes.
8.05      The project would upgrade about 400 km of roads and construct bridges
at two critical river crossings. Additional benefits excluded from the rate
of return are improved farm to market transport conditions which would assist
the entire project area population.
8.06      If the proposed coconut feasibility study confirms that coconut
development is viable, this would provide a basis for diversification in Rivers
State and elsewhere in Southern Nigeria.



- 27 -
