ANNEX I
Page 1
NIGERIA
NUCLEUS ESTATE/SMALLHOLDER OIL PALM PROJECT
RIVERS STATE
OIL PALM SECTOR
A.   Market Prospects for Palm Oil
1.        World Market.  World exports of palm oil, which stagnated through-
out the 1950s and early 1960s, increased at 15% a year after 1967, and by 1975
the share of palm oil in exports of all fats and oils had reached 13% -- the
second largest after soybean oil. The Bank's staff projects world production
of fats and oils to increase by 88% between 1976 and 1985. During this period,
palm oil will expand its share from 6.8 to 10.1%. Most of this will come from
oil palms planted during the late sixties which will reach peak yields during
this period. (New plantings between now and 1980 will not affect palm oil
output significantly before 1985.) Oil palm projects financed by the Bank
Group will contribute about 10.1% to world production of palm oil by 1985.
2.        Internal consumption of palm oil in developing countries is ex-
pected to grow rapidly during the next decade (1976-1985), while palm oil
exports (mainly to developed countries) are projected to grow even faster.
Thus, the share of palm oil in the world trade of fats and oils will rise
from 11.8% in 1976 to 22.9% in 1985. Malaysia will continue to dominate
exports followed by Indonesia: the Ivory Coast and Zaire will be the main
exporters in West Africa.
3.        Developed countries import about 90% of palm oil traded in inter-
national markets. Germany, Netherlands and the United Kingdom account for
almost 60% of world palm oil imports. While the shares of the three Euro-
pean countries declined slightly during the past two decades (1955-1975),
the United States' share in imports rose considerably, and by the early
1970s accounted for 11-12% of the world trade. The rise in U.S. demand
reflects the trend towards greater use of vegetable oils in consumer prod-
ucts and the decline in the price of palm oil relative to that of other
vegetable oils.
4.        Technically, most fats and oils are to some degree interchange-
able. In assessing the market prospects for palm oil, its physical charac-
teristics have to be taken into account. Palm oil is a "hard oil"; it con-
tains a larger portion of saturated fatty acids than most of its major
competitors (soybean oil, sunflower oil, cottonseed oil, etc.), and unlike
the lauric oils (coconut oil and palm kernel oil), does not contain any
components which would guarantee it a market in which it is preferred over
other fats and oils. The supply of palm is highly inelastic, because



ANNEX 1
Page 2
harvesting will continue even if palm oil prices should fall below variable
costs. There are two main reasons: (a) producers have few alternative pro-
duction possibilities, short of uprooting the palms; and (b) they would have
to collect the ripe fresh fruit bunches anyway, to prevent plant diseases
caused by rotting palm fruit. The decision as to whether to extract the oil
will depend largely on the direct operating costs of the mill and the market-
ing costs for the end products (palm oil, palm kernel oil, and palm kernel
cake).
5.        The past decade saw a significant shift in the consumption away
from animal fats and oils to vegetable oils. Among the vegetable oils,
the demand for soft oils (soybean oil, cottonseed oil, sunflower oil, etc,),
expanded more rapidly than the demand for hard oils (palm oil, coconut oil,
palm kernel oil). This trend is expected to continue. By 1985 the share of
vegetable oils in total fats and oils consumption will reach more than 70%.
Continued strong demand for soft oils combined with growing supplies of
hard oils will exert increasing pressure on prices for palm oil and other
hard oils. This has been taken into account in calculating the future eco-
nomic price for palm oil (see Annex 15). Palm oil enjoys one important ad-
vantage over the soft oils however; namely, low production costs which make
it an economically attractive product for many developing countries.
6.        According to Bank projections, world demand for all fats and oils
(based on 1974 constant prices) will rise to 51.7 million tons in 1980 and
57.4 million tons in 1985. Demand for vegetable oils which has grown at an
average rate of 4.2% between 1960 and 1975 is expected to reach about 37 mil-
lion tons in 1980 and 41 million tons in 1985. Meanwhile, world supply of
all fats and oils in 1985 is expected to reach 58.3 million tons. Almost
one-fourth of the total supply will come from soybean oil, about 10% from
palm oil. The total share of vegetable oils (hard oils and soft oils com-
bined) will be 70%, that of animal fats and marine oils, 30%. With world
supply of all fats and oils in 1980 expected to exceed world demand by a
margin of 1.5 million tons, prices for fats and oils (in real terms) are
expected to be around currenLt low levels. Between 1980 and 1985 demand
for fats and oils is projected to grow faster than during 1975-80, reducing
the gap between demand and supplies of fats and oils to 800,000 tons. Thus
prices are projected to recover from their 1980 low levels and to approach
again their level of the late 1960s.
Domestic Market
Supply
7.        During the early sixties Nigeria was one of the world's leading
producers and exporters of palm oil and palm kernels, accounting for about
40% of total production and nearly 3% of world trade. However, from 1960-
1966 annual production remained more or less stagnant. The civil war (1966-
1970) took place mostly in the palm belt of Nigeria which adversely affected
the oil palm industry. Many palm areas (wild groves and estates) and pro-
cessing facilities were then destroyed, large palm areas were cleared for
emergency food production and the whole socio-economic framework of oil palm



ANNEX I
Page 3
production and marketing was disrupted. Since the cessation of hostilities,
production has been increasing but has not yet attained the pre-war level.
8.        The major palm producing areas of Nigeria are located in former
Western, Mid-Western, East Central, South East and the Rivers States. Apart
from some commercial estates, farm settlement and rehabilitated groves, the
bulk of the palm products comes from wild groves. Since most palm oil pro-
duction is either consumed by the farm family or sold in local markets,
statistics on production (and consumption) are subject to considerable
uncertainty. In U.S. Department of Agriculture estimates, Nigerian palm
oil production reached a pre-civil war peak of 580,000 tons in 1966. The
FAO places the peak in 1965, at 574,000 tons. However, one estimate by
the Nigerian Federal Ministry of Agriculture placed pre-war production
at a level closer to 750,000 tons. Production has not returned to pre-war
levels as, according to USDA estimates, the post-war peak to date was achieved
in 1976 with production of 510,000 tons.
9.        What potential is there for further expansion in supply in the ab-
sence of new planting schemes? Nearly 90% of the present supply of palm oil
produced in Nigeria comes from wild groves. However, exploitation of wild
palms reached a maximum in the mid-1960s and any improvements in future ex-
traction rates through the introduction of more efficient processing will be
largely offset by decreased fruits production as trees age and become diffi-
cult to harvest. Production may also be depressed by felling of wild palms
for food crops as population densities increase in the southern states.
10.       As such, the scope for increasing palm oil production from exist-
ing sources is limited and is dependent upon new planting and replanting
programs. Prior to 1966 some plantings of improved palm on estates, farm
settlements and rehabilitated groves had been initiated, but in Eastern
Nigeria the program was halted during the civil war. The study group on
oil palm of the National Agricultural Development Committee estimated in
its report (June 1972) that until 1971/72 about 162,802 acres (66,000 ha)
of palm had been improved. In short, to compare the potential output from
trees already planted with levels achieved before the war one must consider
first the destruction of trees that were bearing in the mid-1960s, and
secondly new plantings (both pre- and post-war) that were not yet yielding
in 1965-66. On this basis it is estimated that total production from exist-
ing plantings, including improved palm planted before the civil war, is
unlikely to exceed 570,000 tons per annum.
11.       Some additional output will come from planting schemes in the
pipeline or under way. The three IBRD-assisted projects in Bendel, Imo
and Ondo States will develop 42,000 hectares with peak annual production
of 98,900 tons of oil. Allowing for implementation delays, their joint
contribution by 1985 may be around 32,000 tons. Another smaller develop-
ment already under way at Ohaji in Imo State may contribute up to 12,000
tons per annum by the mid-1980's. The Federal Military Government plans
to sponsor further new planting and replanting schemes, but it seems un-
likely that these will provide more than 25,000 tons per annum by 1985.
To summarize, estimated Nigerian palm oil production in 1985 might, under



ANNEX 1
Page 4
favorable circumstances, reaclh 640,000 tons without the proposed project,
which would add a further 26,000 tons in 1985 and 55,000 tons at full pro-
duction.
Demand
12.       The domestic market for palm oil is overwhelmingly for household
consumption in preparation of food. Industrial demand for soap and margarine
production has probably averaged less than 20,000 tons per annum, which is
3-4% of total output. Nigeria has ceased to be important on world markets
since the start of the civil war, though there have been small quantities
exported in good years (e.g. 1975). See Tables 1 and 2. 1/
13.       As explained above, the informal processing and marketing arrange-
ments make it impossible to produce precise estimates of output or household
consumption of palm oil within Nigeria.  What is clear, however, is that pres-
ent consumption and production are practically identical.  In other words,
given that there is virtually no inward or outward trade, any revision of
production estimates would have to be accompanied by a similar adjustment
to consumption figures, and vice versa. Working from its own production
estimates and official trade data, the U.S. Department of Agriculture con-
cluded that in 1976 the domestic market would absorb some 485,000 tons.
14.       An alternative approach is to concentrate upon estimating consump-
tion directly, rather than inferring it from production data. The Federal
office of Statistics is presently processing a survey of household consump-
tion patterns, but no results are yet available. The most recent surveys
for which final data are avaiLable date back to the mid-1960s. These gave
an estimated average per capil:a consumption of fats and oils of about 9.4 kg,
of which 90% was accounted for by palm oil. Both of these figures were sub-
ject to considerable regional variation, with the southern states showing
much higher average consumption levels than those in the north. These per
capita consumption figures, a:Lthough valuable, are now markedly out of date;
and aggregation of such per capita figures is itself problematic, in view
of the absence of population statistics that command widespread acceptance.
15.       Although the precise consumption level is difficult to assess,
future demand trends compared to supply potential may be somewhat clearer.
The limited opportunities for expanding supply within the next 10 years have
already been pointed out. On the demand side a major expansion must be ex-
pected, which may lead to further domestic price increases, or to substantial
and increasing imports or both.
1/   The Ministry of Trade permit scheme has apparently been used to restrict
the import of palm oil for consumption but small quantities of special
grade palm oil (2,000-3,000 tons per annum) have been imported in recent
years for industrial use by margarine producers, in the light of the
virtual non-availability of S.P.O. from the NPMC (see Table 2). It has
not been possible to confirm reports of imports of palm oil for consump-
tion purposes.



ANNEX I
Page 5
16.       Firstly, population is expected to continue growing rapidly.  The
Bank forecasts a future growth rate of 2.5% per annum, which appears in line
with recent experience. Average income levels are also expected to continue
their upward trend. The Bank forecasts that real per capita GNP will rise at
6.4% per annum until 1980 and 4.0% between then and 1985.
17.       The relationship between per capita income levels and intake of fats
and oils has been the subject of an extensive cross-country study within the
Bank. It has been concluded that the relationship is not a linear one, but
that the income elasticity of demand for fats and oils is in fact higher at
lower levels of income. In the ranges of average per capita incomes antic-
ipated in Nigeria over the next decade income elasticity of demand can be
expected to be around 0.63. Therefore between 1976 and 1985, aggregate con-
sumption of fats and oils in Nigeria is projected to grow at an average annual
rate of 7.8% from 1976 to 1980 and 5.1% from 1980 to 1985. In aggregate terms,
total consumption of fats and oils is predicted to rise from 565,000 tons in
1976 to 762,000 tons in 1980 and 978,000 tons in 1985.
18.       There remains to be discussed the likely share of palm oil in total
fats and oils demand. Palm oil is the most widely consumed edible oil in
Nigeria, lower in price than its nearest substitutes. Groundnut oil and
cottonseed oil (see paragraphs 19-21) are also consumed, as well as smaller
quantities of other products such as butter, melon-seed oil and coconut oil.
But at the present time it is likely that palm oil accounts for 85-90% of
total fats and oils consumption.
19.       Groundnuts are the second source of edible vegetable oils in Nigeria,
but the quantity of groundnut oil consumed is estimated to be less than 10%
of the consumption of palm oil. Groundnuts are produced in the northern
states, and an important part is consumed directly by farm families. There
is also some "backyard crushing". The Marketing Boards and NPMC have handled
groundnuts for commercial crushing and export. In the past a large propor-
tion of the crop has been exported directly, and in turn most of the commer-
cially milled oil has been exported. Over the past four years the groundnut
crop has been well below earlier levels as a result of climatic conditions
and disease. Many mills have suspended crushing operations, and in 1976 up
to 30,000 tons of groundnut oil were imported to meet domestic requirements.
Details of production and trade appear in Table 6.
20.       Groundnut oil consumption in Nigeria has probably averaged about
40,000 tons per annum in recent years, although the "backyard crushers" make
precise estimates impossible. Groundnut oil is a much higher priced commod-
ity than palm oil, both on international markets, where the price differential
has varied between 45% and 100%, and on the domestic Nigerian market, where
the differential has varied even more widely. Groundnut oil has therefore
been something of a luxury commodity, and while rising income levels may lead
to some substitution at the margin, its higher production costs preclude
groundnut oil's becoming a mass consumption item on the scale of palm oil.



ANNEX I
Page 6
21.       Small quantities of cottonseed oil are also produced and absorbed
by the domestic market (about 20,000 tons a year recently). The seed is a
by-product of the growing ot cotton for the textile industry and most cotton-
seed is used as animal fodder. However, mills have recently been opened that
are capable of crushing both cottonseed and groundnuts. Like groundnut oil,
cottonseed oil is a higher priced commodity than palm oil.
22.       In view of their status as luxury products some substitution towards
competing fats and oils and away from palm oil is to be expected. It is as-
sumed, therefore, that palm oil's share of the market will fall from 85-90%
at present to 80-85% by the mid-1980s. In summary, it is estimated that the
potential domestic market for palm oil by 1985 will be in the range of
780,000-830,000 tons per annum.
23.       The estimates of domestic demand and supply presented above lead
to the conclusion that a shortfall of some 140,000-190,000 tons of palm oil
may be expected in 1985 in the absence of further measures to expand output.
24.       Palm kernels.  Approximately 90% of kernel production has been pur-
chased by NPMC, through the Marketing Boards, for sale to local processors
or for export. Until the mid-1960's, there was very little local crushing
of kernels, and nearly the entire production was exported in kernel form.
Since 1965, however, several crushing mills have been established, and in
recent years have absorbed about a third of total kernel production, the rest
being exported directly. In turn, about half of the kernel oil from these
local mills has tended to go for export (see Table 4).
25.       The Federal Military Government has responsibility for setting the
producer price paid to farmers, and also the resale price to domestic crushers.
As Table 5 shows, NPMC's prices usually lagged well behind export parity
prices, and this led to difficulties after 1975 when world market prices for
kernels (and kernel oil) slumped. Local kernel crushers were still paying
boom prices for their kernel supplies, while their export sales prices for oil
had fallen very sharply. While awaiting a price review by FMG, several of the
Nigerian crushing mills were obliged to suspend operations.
Institutional Framework
26.       The majority of palm oil is produced by local methods and either
consumed at home or sold in village markets, by-passing official marketing
channels. The standard retail unit is the beer bottle (0.65 liter). Retail
and wholesale prices are subject to considerable variation, but seem to be
well above official marketing board purchase prices or indeed a probable
import parity level. Prices in Port Harcourt in July-September 1975 were
25-30 kobos per beer bottle, while prices for a 4 gallon tin were said to
fluctuate between N 10 and N 14. The quantities handled officially have
accordingly fallen to a very small proportion of output (see Tables 2 and 3).
27.       The declining importance of marketing boards is also a reflection
of the decline in Nigeria's status as a significant exporter of palm oil.
The official bodies have enjoyed a monopoly of sales of palm oil and kernels



ANNEX I
Page 7
for export and local processsing, and while these markets are still of major
importance in the case of kernels, they now account for only a trivial pro-
portion of palm oil sales. Unable to match the prices that producers can
obtain in local markets, the marketing boards' oil supplies have in recent
years been mostly limited to output from a few government-owned plantations
and mills.
28.       The official marketing system until recently consisted of a market-
ing board in each state. In the case of Rivers State this function was
performed by the Agricultural Production and Marketing Corporation (see Annex
2). The marketing boards were responsible for purchasing agricultural produce
including palm oil and palm kernels and other commercial crops. 1/ Produce
was purchased through locally appointed Licensed Buying Agents, operating
on a fiscal margin per ton of producer known as a block buying allowance.
The purchasing prices have been fixed annually by the Head of the Federal
Military Government, following the abandonment of earlier arrangements under
which the marketing boards themselves fixed these prices.
29.       At the federal level the marketing boards were associated with the
Nigerian Produce Marketing Company, which enjoyed a monopoly of sales for
export and sales to domestic processors. The above organizational structure
was replaced in April 1977 by a new system of commodity boards. Each board is
being established on a national basis and will take over the present responsi-
bilities of the NPMC and the marketing boards for one particular commodity or
group of commodities. Palm oil and kernels are among the commodities to be
handled by the new Palm Produce Board.
1/   Cocoa, groundnuts, benniseed, coffee, copra, cotton, grapefruit,
kenaf, sheanut, and soybeans.

RIVERS STATE
STATE INSTITUTIONS
1.        The Ministry of Trade and Economic Development is responsible for
coordinating development proposals within Rivers State. Five-year plans are
drafted at state level, and finalized after submission to the federal author-
ities. The current plan period runs from 1975 to 1980. The original plan
document envisaged a five-year program of N 472 million, of which 10.1% was
earmarked for agriculture, plus a further 2.8% for forestry, fisheries and
livestock. The new State Administration is now reviewing all programs in
the plan.
2.        The Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources (MANR) is respon-
sible for formulating and executing agriculture, livestock, fisheries and
forestry development programs. It also operates research programs, and
supervises the work of the School of Agriculture and Fisheries.
3.        The Extension Division of MANR has a small headquarters staff of 9
senior and intermediate level personnel under the Chief Agricultural Officer.
The field staff is organized geographically, with the state divided into 6
zones. Staff in each zone should be headed by a Senior Agricultural Officer
(or Senior Fisheries Officer) but currently these posts are filled by staff
of Agricultural Officer level. The total provision for senior and inter-
mediate level staff for the Divison is 278 posts, but in October 1976 only
176 posts were filled. The shortfall is at the intermediate levels of Agri-
cultural Superintendents, Agricultural Assistants and Field Overseers.
4.        Extension activities of oil palm development to date have been
limited to distribution of NIFOR improved planting material. The program
has suffered from delays and wastage of planting material, and does not
provide the intensive technical assistance and follow-up required.
5.        Since the civil war there has been little opportunity for MANR
staff to gain experience operating agricultural credit schemes. In March
1976, however, a Supervised Agricultural Credit scheme was formally launched
by the Ministry, with an allocation of N 500,000 for the year 1976-77. The
purpose of the scheme is to provide small loans, mostly in kind, with annual
interest at 2%; the loan repayment period depends on the crop, with tree
crop loans having the longest term. A new section has been formed within
MANR to operate the credit scheme, and the aim is to allocate at least one
credit officer to each of the former administrative divisions. Although
loan application forms have been distributed, in October 1976 no applica-
tions had been processed, and no loans granted.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
6.        Training in agriculture within the state is provided by two insti-
tutions. The MANR's own School of Agriculture and Fisheries at Onne operates
a one-year crash training program, and reported an intake of 110 students in
1976. The School of Agriculture, Fisheries and Forestry in the College of
Science and Technology, Port Harcourt, offers an eight-term diploma course
in general agriculture, comprising six terms of course work and two of prac-
tical farming. The student population in mid-1976 was reported as 142. Both
institutions have experienced difficulties over physical facilities, and the
MANR School also suffers from a lack of permanent teaching staff.
Agricultural Production and Marketing Corporation
7.        APMC was the result of a merger between the Rivers State Marketing
Board (see Annex 1) and the Rivers State Development Corporation in August
1974. In consequence, APMC had a dual role, that of a marketing board, pur-
chasing commodities through Licensed Buying Agents, and that of a direct pro-
ducer. In the latter role, APMC operated estates and mills on behalf of the
State Government, including the present Elele Oil Palm Plantation, a number
of palm oil mills, and several rubber plantations.
8.        APMC was reorganized in April 1977, when the marketing board system
was changed (Annex 1). The Marketing responsibilities passed to the Palm
Produce Board, and the direct production role was divided between two newly
established companies, one of which assumed all assets in the rubber sector,
while the other - Risonpalm Ltd. - took over the assets and management of
State interests in oil palm, including the proposed project. Details of
Risonpalm Ltd. are in Annex 10. As existing APMC management personnel do not
possess the capability and experience to implement new planting schemes senior
staff for the proposed project will be recruited locally and internationally.
Cooperatives
9.        The Rivers State cooperative movement consists of primary societies
(507 at 6/30/75), secondary unions (8 local cooperative unions and coopera-
tive credit unions) and three apex organizations at state level. Legal steps
required to form a cooperative are minimal. After an application has been
approved, the cooperative becomes an unregistered society; it can be fully
registered after satisfactory performance under supervision. Of the 507
primary societies, 289 were registered and 218 unregistered.
10.       In the four divisions most affected by the proposed project the
number of registered primary societies, and their membership as of late 1976
were as given in the table below. Many other societies were still at the
"'unregistered" stage. It would assist project implementation if members of
unregistered societies, for which applications had already been approved,
could be incorporated in the credit scheme.



ANNEX 2
Page 3

Ikwerre                            23                              678
Ahoada                             30                              861
Etche                              11                              524
Abua/Odual                         13                              334
Total                   77                            2,397
11.       Of the three apex boclies, the Rivers State Cooperative Produce
Exporters Ltd. acts as a licensed buying agent for APMC handling palm kernel
and cocoa output of the primary societies. The second apex organization,
the Rivers State Cooperative Supply and Consumers Association Ltd. performs
a wholesaling function, supplying commodities to primaries for resale. The
third apex body is the Rivers State Cooperative Union Ltd. which acts as a
spokesman for the interests of the state cooperative movement.
12.       In contrast to many other states, there is no cooperative bank or
financing society in Rivers State. The State Government has allocated N 1.5
million for funding a cooperative bank, but is awaiting a Federal Government
review of the financial system. This is expected to result in a definition of
policy for development of the banking system, which will enable the State Gov-
ernment to decide whether to establish a bank or a financing society. The
latter would be easier, since a financing society may be registered by the
Registrar of Cooperative Societies under the Cooperatives Ordinance with a
minimum of formalities, while a bank must satisfy the more stringent Banking
Ordinance provisions including a minimum capitalization of N 600,000. In
either case, the members of the bank or financing society would be the primary
cooperative societies, which could hold accounts with and receive loans from
apex organizations.
13.       The Cooperative Division of the Ministry of Rural Development and
Social Welfare is responsible for regulating the cooperative movement. Each
society's books are supposed to be inspected by the division every six months.
Before the civil war, there operated a system of traveling secretaries who
assisted the primary societies with bookkeeping, but this has not been re-
established. The Cooperative Division has been hampered by a shortage of
staff and in late 1976, the Registrar reported a limited professional staff
of 2 assistant registrars, 5 cooperative officers and 5 assistant coopera-
tive officers. Until recently, salaries within the Cooperative Division
have not been attractive compared with similar Government positions although
a recent adjustment should lead to some improvement in staff recruitment and
retention.
14.       The project would strengthen the cooperative movement, both through
the financial resources that it would channel to the primary societies (see
Annex 5) and also through the technical assistance that the credit staff of
the SHU would provide to the officers of the cooperative societies in the
project area. The credit officers and loan assistants would work closely with
those societies whose members participated in the project, and SMU would co-
ordinate its activities with those of the cooperative division.

RIVERS STATE
TECHNICAL ASPECTS OF ESTABLISHMENT AND PRODUCTION
A.   Climate, Soils, Vegetation, Pests and Diseases in Project Areas
1.        It has been shown that the main influence on the bunch yield of the
oil palm in West Africa is the severity of the dry season two years previously
and that the general level of yield for any soil class is related to the water
deficit. 1/
Rainfall and Water Deficit
2.        The rainfall of Southern Nigeria and particularly of Rivers State
is shown on Map 10562R1 attached to this Annex. It will be noted that the
project lies in an area with a mean annual rainfall of + 2,500 mm (100").
This annual rainfall increases rapidly to the South of the project area
while it decreases slowly towards the North. It is usually considered
ample for the oil palm if the monthly distribution is even.
3.        A feature of the rainfall pattern in Rivers State is a single long
rainy season from March to mid-November. From March onward the rainfall in-
creases to a peak in September (the mean for 11 years in AHOADA is 500 mm
with annual variations ranging from 280 to 700 mm), and then decreases rapidly
in October and November. The dry season (including months with less than 100
mm) lasts 3 to 4 months from mid-November to the end of February. It should
be noted, however, that annual rainfall varies considerably from year to year,
this being a general feature of the climate in West Africa.
4.        The water deficit not only varies with the annual rainfall, it also
depends on the monthly rainfall distribution. The water deficits shown in Map
2 have been calculated by a standard I.R.H.O. formula which assumes a soil
water reserve of 200 mm and an evapo-transpiration of 150 mm in months with
less than 10 days' rain, and an evapo-transpiration of 120 mm in months with
10 rainy days or more. Water deficits vary considerably from year to year
and this is reflected in the marked variation in annual bunch yields charac-
teristic of planted areas in Nigeria.
1/   Annual Report - institut de Recherches pour les Huiles et les Oleagineux
(I.R.H.O.) 1966.



ANNEX 3
Page 2
5.        Map 10561R1 shows the iso-deficit lines estimated from the water
deficits of the stations marked. These water deficits are related to two
periods: 1950-1962 and the early seventies. The Map shows that the proj-
ect lies in areas having a mean annual water deficit between 200 and 250 mm.
These water deficits are among the lowest in West Africa.
Temperatures
6.        Mean monthly temperatures in AHOADA, situated in the project area,
lie between 26oC and 28oC with mean maxima varying between 29oC and 36oC and
mean minima between 2OoC and 23oC. The highest temperatures are experienced
at the end of the dry season and the beginning of the rainy season.  The
mean monthly minimum temperature always exceeds 18oC which is considered
to be a critical minimum temperature for oil palm cultivation. There is no
inhibiting effect on the growth of the oil palm and mean temperatures are
very close to 28oC which is considered to be the optimum for growth and
production.
Humidity
7.        Mean monthly humidity is always very high, especially in the rainy
season.   The effect of the harmattan wind during the dry season is probably
very limited as the relative humidity is fairly steady during this period.
This factor can be considered as being satisfactory for oil palm cultivation.
Sunshine and Sky Radiation
8.        Sunshine records in Rivers State are only kept for Port Harcourt
airport which is situated 5 km south of the project area. On the basis of
the limited data available, it is estimated that the mean annual sunshine is
about 1,400 hours. For a long time, sunshine of 1,800 hours was considered
a prudent minimum for the optimum photosynthetic activity, whilst 1,500 hours
was accepted as the lowest limit. Recently, however, the IRHO noted that
there is no direct relationship between sunshine (recorded with a Campbell
Stokes) and the actual sky radiation (cal/cm/day) which controls photosyn-
thetic activity. The following data assembled by the I.R.H.O. and quoted
by SOCFINCO in its feasibility study confirm this assumption.
Sunshine       Sky Radiation         Yield/ha
hour/year        cal./sq.cm.         tons FFB
Ivory Coast (I.C.)                1,850              335                16
Ecuador (E)                         900              215                18
Ratio (E/I.C.)                        0.49             0.79
River State
(Port Harcourt)              1,400/1,500          315
Ratio (R.S./I.C.)                     0.76             0.94
9.        The yields of 18 tons/FFB year are obtained in Ecuador despite the
low sunshine, but on very good soils and with a rainfall which is satisfac-
tory on the whole, albeit very badly distributed. The above data show that



ANNEX 3
Page 3
a total of 1,400 hours annual sunshine certainly has no inhibiting effect on
growth and production but as a precaution, a coefficient of correction of 95%
(Ratio R.S./I.C. for sky radiation) is applied to the potential yield deduced
from the relationship between production and water deficit (Figure 1).
Topography
10.       The largest part of the project area is situated on the Coastal
Plain Terrace (Pliocene - Pleistocene - Eocene - Oligocene - Miocene) which
covers a very large part of Southern Nigeria. This plain is of low altitude
and slopes gently towards the south. The topography is flat or very slightly
undulating; a few small swamps are encountered mostly in the southern part.
Only four rivers (Sombrero, Otamiri plus its tributary, and Imo Rivers) cross
the plain and flow to the south. The southern part of the smallholder area
is more dissected by small rivers which flow towards the Tidal Flats (Mangrove
area).
11.       The rest of the project area is situated on the Sub Recent Niger
Terrace (early Holocene - late Pleistocene) which occupies an intermediate
position between the Coastal Plain and the Delta Plain. This terrace is also
very flat but due to its lower altitude it is dissected by a network of smaller
streams and creeks fringed by narrow strips of swampy areas. The relief of the
project area is very suitable for the layout of the Nucleus Estate (plots of
100 ha) and road construction would be easy. In the smallholder area it would
be necessary to build some bridges over the main rivers and to install culverts
across the streams in the Sub Recent Terrace.
Soils
Soils of the Coastal Plain Terrace or "Acid Sands"
12.       These soils are classed in the ferrallitic soils (French system) or
in the Oxisols order (U.S.D.A. system). The parent materials are unconsoli-
dated grits and sandstones. According to Vine they are mainly classified
in the yellow CALABAR FASC. Typically these soils are deep, homogenous, free
of coarse elements (particles above 2 mm), never waterlogged (except in swampy
depressions). The dominant color is generally dark brown becoming clearer
and brighter down the profile as the clay content increases. The consistency
is friable, slightly or not at all plastic, and sticky. Internal drainage
is satisfactory.
13.       SOCFINCO and I.R.H.O. pedologists surveyed a zone of 68,000 ha
(1,000 km of traverses were cut) which included the nucleus estate sites for
phase I and phase 2 and a small proportion of the smallholder areas. During
this survey different soil series were identified according to their fine
particle content at 2 ft level (60-80 cm). Generally the top soil (above
60 cm) is sand, loamy sand, or sandy loam or even sandy clay loam while the
sub soil (below 60 cm) varies from sandy loam to heavy sandy clay loam.



ANNEX 3
Page 4
14.       The main features of the classification in soil series are briefly
described below:
Yellow-brown Plateau Soils
Light sandy clay series (sandy loam) of EGBEDA. This soil
series occupied only a very limited sector in the North
West part of the smallholder area. Fine particle content
at 2 ft level: 15-20%.
Sandy clay series (sandy loam) of ELELE occupies a large
area in the North-West part of the Nucleus Estate (Phase
1). Fine particle content at 2 ft level: 20-30%; clay
content at 2 ft level: +25%.
Heavy sandy clay series (sandy clay loam) of OBELE is not
most representative of the Nucleus Estate (Phase 2). Fine
particle content at 2 ft level: 35-42%; clay content at
2 ft level: +35%.
Red-brown Plateau Soils
Light clay sandy series (sandy clay loam) of OMERULU occupies
almost all the North-East Part of the Nucleus Estate (Phase 1)
and is also represented in the North-West part. Fine parti-
cle content at 2 ft. deep: 23-38%; clay content at 2 ft
deep: +25%.
15.       The soil maps contained in the SOCFINCO study give the geographi-
cal location of the different soils series - these maps cover a small part
of the smallholder area which was too large to be surveyed with traverses.
But reconnaissances were made by SOCFINCO and by the mission along the
roads (smallholdings would be developed within strips 600 m wide along
the roads). These reconnaissances showed that similar types of soil occur.
16.       A high proportion of the balance between total earth and fine
particle content consists of coarse sands which provide good drainage and
give a friable consistency to the soils, favorable to root development.
SOCFINCO also give some data on the water holding capacity of the soils
which compares very satisfactorily with that of similar soils of other
areas in West Africa where oil palm grows well.
17.       The soils are acid and the organic matter content is fairly high
for these soil types. The total phosphorus content is very high but the
potassium levels are very low and the magnesium levels are low for oil palm
cultivation. The base saturation is low or medium; this is very common in
the rainy equatorial zone.
18.       In conclusion it can be said that the physical characteristics of
the Coastal Plain Sands are quite suitable for oil palm cultivation but that
the potassium and magnesium deficiencies would have to be made good by fer-
tilizer.



ANNEX 3
Page 5
Soils of the Sub-Recent Niger Terrace
19.       The most frequent type of soil encountered in the flat areas of
the part of the Sub-Recent Terrace included in the smallholders area is
rather similar to the lighter series of the Coastal Plain Sands. The top
soil is dark brown on top and then brown, while the sub-soil is mainly yel-
low but can also be brown or beige. Generally, the top soil is a sandy
loam or a sandy clay loam with a clay content ranging from 15 to 25% and
a low silt content. There can be some variations in the silt content; a
few profiles have been identified as silty sandy loam. All these soils
are suitable for oil palm cultivation.
20.       The strips of waterlogged soils along the little streams and some
patches of very sandy soils encountered (during the reconnaissance carried
out at the time of the mission) along the Western border of the smallholder
areas should be rejected. The S.M.U. field officers would have to cross-
check the physical soil characteristics during their visits to the fields
when the farmers applied to join the scheme.
21.       There are no chemical data for the soils of the Sub-Recent Niger
Terrace, but due to the general effect of weathering and leaching in the
equatorial zones these soils can be expected to be low in potash and
magnesium.
Vegetation
22.        In the Eastern area of the Nucleus Estate (phase 1), where the popu-
lation is scanty, the vegetation consists mainly of oil palm at different den-
sities (a few up to more than 100/ha) mixed with fallow at various stages of
regrowth. A high proportion of this sector is under secondary regenerated
jungle and includes trees of medium size and height (umbrella trees) at a
rather high density. Some remnants of the original rain forest are also
encountered in this sector.

in percentage of the total area:

According to the percentage of land under food crops the mean turnover period
is approximately 12 years, but some ateas may be more frequently farmed than
others.

23.       In the Western area of the Nucleus Estate, also sparsely populated,
the density of wild nalms is generally high but there are fewer trees than
on the Eatern side. ihe area under food crops is also small.
Nucleus Estate, Western Sector: distribution of the various vegetation types
of vegetation in percentage of the total area:
Forest remnants                                  5
Secondary regenerated jungle                    33
Fallow (regrowth ages 2-3 years)                50
Tree crops                                     1
Food crops                                    11
Total                       100
According to the percentage of land under food crops the mean turnover period
is approximately 9 years.
24.       In the smallholding areas where population density is high, the
proportion of land under cultivation increases, the fallow is younger and
the density of trees, including oil palms, is lower. The most advanced stage
of exploitation of the original rain forest is found in the Southern and
Eastern sector (east of the OTAMIRI River) of the project areas where few
trees remain. In those areas the land is under food crops (mostly cassava
and yams) and young fallow.
25.       Eupatorium Odoratum 1/ is present in all recently cleared areas.
It invades the fields very rapidly after harvest and is the dominant species
in young fallows.
Pests and Diseases of Oil Palm in Rivers State
Pests
26.       Coelaenomenodera Elaeidis (Leaf Miner) is a permanent hazard in
West Africa and a severe outbreak occurred in Cowan Estate (Bendel State)
in 1967. It was identified in the ELELE Plantation during the Appraisal
Mission. Generally there is an effective biological control; the intro-
duction of some species of the parasite fauna will help this control.
Spraying with Parathion can 'be used in the case of severe outbreaks - the
I.R.H.O. is currently studying control with systemic insecticides.
27.       Oryctes (Rhinoceros beetle).  The population is small in Rivers
State. Damage to coconut is very limited. A cover crop overgrowing the
decaying trunks of oil palm will prevent the beetles laying their eggs and
multiplying. In case of infestation, ethyl chrysanthemate traps can be
placed on the plantings.
1/   A noxious weed which adversely affects oil palm establishment.



ANNEX 3
Page 7
28.       Root Borers.  The caterpillar of a Coleoptera beetle bores into
the roots which are destroyed by the subsequent rot. This pest has been found
near BORI. The I.-R.H.O. is studying its control in the Cameroons.
29.       Leaf eating caterpillars.  Various species have been observed in
Nigeria but generally they do not cause severe damage and they can be con-
trolled with insecticides.
30.       Other insects - Rhynchophorus Phoenicis, a palm weevil, can cause
damage when rodents are not controlled; Temnoschoita quadripustulata is also
present but is seldom a serious pest in nurseries.
31.       Rodents (rats and cutting-grass - Agoutis) are abundant and can
cause severe damage to young palms. The latter are protected against the
rats by collars of wire netting and also with baits made of poison. Cutting-
grass are killed by hunters.
Diseases
32.       Fusarium Oxysporum (Vascular Wilt) was already present in ELELE
plantation in 1974. Muriate of potassium can slow down the spread of the
disease. It is recommended that Wilt tolerant planting material is used
for new plantings in areas where Wilt has been identified.
33.       Cercospora and Anthracnose were reported in neglected nurseries
near BORI. They can be controlled with Dithane, Benlate, Pelt and Bavistine.
Sometimes it may be necessary to spray fungicides during the first years in
the fields.
34.       Blast.  The losses resulting from Blast in the nurseries are diffi-
cult to determine in Nigeria. In other countries of West Africa the annual
losses vary a lot, but in some years they can be as high as 70-80%. The
I.R.H.O. recommends shading of the nurseries during the dry season. Losses
due to blast in on-going Bank project nurseries have not been severe in spite
of a policy of no shade. It must however, be emphasized that in these
nurseries sprinkler or mobile irrigators have been in use. SOCFINCO pro-
pose using shade in the dry season until convinced that the incidence of
blast can be fully controlled by irrigation alone.



ANNEX 3
Page 8

36.       The yield of this new planting material is much higher than that
of the Extension Work Seed produced in the previous years, but its Wilt
tolerance has as yet not been verified.
37.       The water deficit is 330 mm at NIFOR where, as can be seen above,
the estimated yield is 12.4 tons FFB/ha. Figure 1 shows that in the Ivory
Coast, by reason of the relationship between water deficit and yield, the
latter increases from 14 tons/ha to almost 17 tons (20%) when the deficit
falls from 330 mm to 250 mm (the maximum water deficit in the project area
in Rivers Sate). Assuming the same relationship for NIFOR planting mate-
rial in the conditions of Rivers State, and applying a correction factor
of 95% to allow for lower sky radiation, the potential yield would be 12.4
tons x 120% corrected x 95% = +14 tons/ha when the estate reached peak pro-
duction.  The oil/bunch ratio is unaffected by climate.

The yield for smallholdings is expected to be lower because most of the farms
would be planted in heavily cultivated areas and because experience has shown
that husbandry standards are not as high on small farms as on well managed
estates.
C. PROPOSED CULTIVATION TECHNIQUES
39.       To obtain well established early bearing palms in West Africa cer-
tain basic practices must be followed. The more important of these being:
(a) to use seeds of high-yielding planting materials (hybrids
between Dura Deli and African Pisifera);
(b) to raise the seedlings in two-stage polybag nurseries with
correct manuring and watering;
(c) to carry out vigorous culling of all abnormal or poorly-devel-
oped seedlings at the end of both nursery stages (pre-and main);
(d) to fell, burn and windrow the vegetation when preparing
the land. It may also be necessary to plough in order
to eradicate noxious weeds such as Eupatorium odoratum;
the aim being to have clean planting sites with easy
access to the young palms;
(e) to sow an adequate quantity of cover crop seeds;
(f) to field plant the seedlings as early as possible in the
rainy season (April-May in Rivers State conditions);
(g) to sow pueraria in late February, early March; to control
the vegetation by circle weeding, and to eliminate harm-
ful weeds in the interrow, especially during the first
years after planting;
(h) to ensure adequate manuring, whatever the age of the palms;
(i) to ensure the palms enjoy good phytosanitary conditions.
Unless these requirements are met fully, the yields expected in this project
will not be attained.
Germination
40.       The seeds would be germinated by NIFOR in water-heated or electri-
cally operated germinators. The "Dry Heat" method now commonly used ensures
at least 80-85% germination. The process takes approximately 100 days. The
germinated seeds - in the first stage of differentiation between pulmule and
radicle known as the "white point" stage - would be delivered to the Project



ANNEX 3
Page 10
packed in special containers to avoid any damage and losses during transport.
Normally it is assumed that 250 germinated seeds are required per ha to be
planted.
Pre-Nurseries
41.       The two-stage nursery in polybags (pre-nursery and main nursery)
is recommended because it makes supervision of the very young seedlings
easier during the first months after sprouting. The following are recom-
mendations for nursery equipment and practices:
(a) the polybags of 200 gauge (average size H 20 cm x W 10 cm
laid flat, with 20 holes pierced in the lower part for
drainage) should be filled with top soil;
(b) the top soil (0-20 cm) should be collected under forest or
in old fallow, sieved and disinfected (2 g Aldrin - 1 g
Cryptonol in 10 1 water for 200 bags) 2 weeks prior to
planting; two weeks after planting the bags should be
treated again with Cryptonol alone (1 g for 10 1);
(c) the germinated seeds should be planted from December through
February with the radicle downwards and at a depth of 2 cm;
(d) a shading cover should be provided, made of palm fronds
placed on a wooden frame to protect the seedlings; the
pre-nursery should be surrounded by a wire fence;
(e) the bags should usually be hand-weeded, but could be
weeded chemically before the plumule sprouts with a
solution of 0.2% Ametryne (Gesapax);
(f) there should be regular watering (2 mm/day);
(g) a monthly fertilizer dressing (type 12-12-20) should be
given at the rate of 100 g in 10 1 water for 4000 seed-
lings; the bags must be well watered just afterwards
to avoid burns;
(h) regular pest and disease control measures should be
practiced to avoid insect proliferation (Dieldrin,
Carbaryl, Dimethoate) and to prevent fungus diseases
(Dithane, Pelt, Benlate);
(i) Culling of all abnormal seedlings should be done before
transplanting to the main nursery at the 4-leaf stage;
losses due to culling can be estimated at 20%;
(j) the standards are:
- approximately 4 m2 of pre-nursery per ha planted;
-  length of pre-nursery period 3 to 4 month.



ANNEX 3
Page 11
Main Nursery
42.        Given that distances are not too great (over 30 km) it is easier
and more convenient to have only one central nursery, the site being well
levelled, drained and weeded (manually or chemically).
-    the nursery should be surrounded by wire netting;
-    the polybags of 500 gauge (average size 50 cm R1-  40 cm W
laid flat with 50 holes perforated in the lower part for
drainage), should be filled with top soil and given basic
fertilizer dressing dissolved in water; the soil must be
well tamped down in the bag when filling;
-    the bag should be placed before the seedlings are trans-
planted.
43.        The effect of Blast has not been closely studied in Nigeria, but as
the disease exists in most West African countries it is provisionally recom-
mended that, as a precaution, the nursery be shaded during the dry season
(October to the end of February) with palm fronds laid over a wood and bamboo
frame. This cover should be sprayed with Carbaryl to stop caterpillars de-
stroying it. To reduce the cost of shade, bags should be placed at the be-
ginning of the nursery period at a spacing of 40 x 40 cm in beds of 5 rows
of bags (45,000 seedlings per ha of nursery); in mid-February when the Blast
season is over, the shade should be removed over two weeks, half the first
and half the second, and the bags placed at 70 x 70 cm to allow for growth
(17,000 seedlings per ha of nursery); special arrangements such as alternate
bands with and without shade reduce handling when the plants are put out at
the wider spacing.
44.       Transplanting of pre-nursery seedlings takes place from mid-April
through June; care must be taken not to earth up the root bulb and to ensure
good contact between the earth ball and soil in the bag.
45.       Watering is essential during the dry season and should be done at
least every other day. The monthly quantity varies with the age of the
seedlings; when they are more than 6 months old the quantity of water should
be the equivalent of 180 mm of rain/month. Mechanical irrigation with the
aid of sprinkles or by self propelled irrigation systems ensures even dis-
tribution of the water and is the most economical method for large nurseries
(an advantage of central nurseries).
46.       Weeding can either be done manually as often as necessary or chem-
ically if qualified staff is available. For chemical weeding the following
can be used:
(a) before the bags are placed, an inexpensive total weed-
killer (sodium chlorate at the rate of 15 kg/ha) to clean
the soil;



ANNEX 3
Page 12
(b)  to treat the substratum in the bags, Ametryne (Gesapax 80)
can be used as follows:
- 2-4 weeks after transplanting, spray with a 0.2%
solution of Ametryne at the rate of 1,500 1/ha of
nursery;
- 4 months after transplanting, spray with a 0.4%
solution of Ametryne at the rate of 1,500 1/ha of
nursery.
(c) fungus control: Dithane (200 g/100 1) or Pelt (150 g/1,100 1)
or Benlate (100 g/100 1) against Cercospora and Anthracnose;
(d) pest control: Dieldrine (300 g/100 1) against Temnoshoista
and termintes, Carbaryl (100 g/100 1) against leaf eaters,
Dimethoate (40 g/100 1) against scales, baits for rodents.
47.       The fertilizer rates depend on the fertility of the top soil used
to fill the bags. The following average rates can be recommended:

Land Preparation
Nucleus Estate
50.       The vegetation cover varies from place to place (see para Vegetation),
which means that the method of land prepration would vary as well; however, the
general lines are as follows:
In forest remnants and secondary regenerated jungle areas where
Eupatorium is less abundant:
(a) manual underbrushing;
(b) the trees would be felled with crawler tractors equipped
with KG blades and tree pushers. A 300 h.p. tractor will
be used where the vegetation is heaviest, but very often
a 140 h.p. tractor will be sufficient, the assistance of
teams with chain saws being provided if necessary;
(c) the biggest logs should be cut into 4-6 m sections with
chain saws;
(d) the vegetation should be burnt during the dry season;
(e) after burning the remaining timbers would be windrowed
with 140 h.p. tractor;
(f) if Eupatorium appears in the interrows it should be era-
dicated before the cover crop is sown. Some trials using
herbicides prior to sowing covers have been carried out
but the results to date are not conclusive. Given this
situation, it is assumed that regenerated growth of
Eupatorium in the interrows will be removed by two cross
ploughings using a Rome Plow TRH followed by stacking
and burning. Reinfestation is always liable to occur
even in areas of dense vegetation, as this weed is present
along the roads and in young fallows.
51.       In areas where the wild palm grove is dominant (North-West side)
the land clearing methods will be similar but more spraying or ploughing may
have to be done. The palms provide less shade and light encourages the
growth of Eupatorium.



ANNEX 3
Page 14
52.       In areas already cleared (fields or young fallows) ploughing will be
necessary as spraying may not be effective. When the density of trees and old
stumps is low, it might be possible to plough after underbrushing and before
tree felling.
Smallholders
53.       The plant cover varies more than on the Nucleus Estate site.  The
majority of the individual farms will be in areas of palm groves, fallows and
crops (see para Vegetation). The main problem will be the eradication of
Eupatorium. Unless spraying by special teams of the S.M.U. proves effective 1/
Eupatorium rhizomes will have to be removed by digging out manually. Satis-
factory elimination of Eupatorium will be one condition for the payment of
credit to the farmers.
54.       It is recommended that the S.M.U. organizes chain-saw gangs to assist
farmers in felling and clearing land prior to sowing covers. Farmers would be
charged at cost which is estimated to be N 46.35 per ha compared with N 105 per
ha if manually felled and cleared by farmers.
Cover Crop
55.       Pueraria javanica is the legume most frequently used in West Africa
as a cover crop. Its main purpose is to fix nitrogen and prevent the re-
establishment of harmful weeds such as Eupatorium, assuming that they have
been completely eliminated prior to the planting of Pueraria. There is prac-
tically no risk of erosion in Rivers State. According to some observations
made in OHAJI (Imo State) on the Ada-palm Estate, it is necessary to use
10-12 kg Pueraria seed per ha; with this quantity, the legume covers the
terrain very quickly and the labor requirements and costs for subsequent
weeding are lower.
Field Planting
56.       Plantings made early in the rainy season are more precocious, there-
fore field planting should be done from April to mid June only. Late plantings
in September and October are not recommended. The seedlings would be planted
at a 9 m triangular spacing at a density of 143 trees/ha.
57.       The earth ball should be damped in the nursery prior to transport-
ing to the field to stop it breaking, and a good contact between the ball and
the surrounding soil must be ensured by packing earth in the gap (without
pressing on the ball, however); the root bulb should not be buried. Careful
planting is vital if the young plants are to take well.
1/   It is proposed that the S.M.U. carries out screening trials with herbi-
cides during the first two years of the project.



ANNEX 3
Page 15
58.       It is recommended that a wire guard should be placed around each
plant at the moment of planting as a protection against rodents. The net-
ting does not offer complete protection, but it avoids heavy losses during
the first 18 months in the field; its removal at the end of nl must not be
forgotten.
Field Establishment - Sequence of Operations
59.       Figure 2 gives a bar chart of the sequence of operation during years
n-2, n-I and nO. The rains prevent any mechanical work from June to end of
October.
Maintenance of the Young Plantings (nO - n6)
60.       Standard maintenance:  a circle is kept clean 1 ft beyond the per-
pendicular of the frond tips; selective weeding in the interrows to stop re-
growth of weeds; pruning limited to the removal of dried leaves when bearing
starts. Supplying of missing plants in nO and nl. Special care must be taken
to avoid reinfestation by Eupatorium; continuous patrolling by special teams
can be very effective on the Estate, but smallholders will require strict
supervision by S.M.U. field staff and payment of credit must only be made if
maintenance work is satisfactory.
Ablation
61.       This operation, which consists of excising the first inflorescences
when the young spathes are developing, can only be carried out on the Estates.
It is started when 50% of the trees have young inflorescences and stopped
towards the middle of n3 when the trees have a mean girth of 5 ft. This in-
creases initial bunch weight and concentrates the first production in a shorter
period of time without any loss of total production.
Maintenance of Mature Plantings
62.       Hand weeding of the circles (radius 60") in the smallholdings, and
the possibility of chemical ring weeding from n7 on in the Estate. Slashing
and selective weeding of the interrow vegetation replacing the Pueraria from
n6/n7 onwards. Pruning should leave at least one green frond under a ripening
bunch.
Harvesting
63.       Standard harvesting technique:  small, narrow-bladed chisel from n3
to n5, to avoid cutting the green fronds; then a short-handled axe or pole
knife from n6 to nIO; finally a pole knife of a length adapted to the height
of the trees from nlO onwards.
Phytosanitary Control
64.       Throughout the life of the plantation and particularly in the early
years periodical inspections must be made so that attacks by pests (rodents,
Oryctes, Rhyncophorus, caterpillars, etc.) and diseases can be discovered in



ANNEX 3
Page 16
time. On Estates this check is made by the estate staff, whilst in the small-
holdings it should be done by the S.M.U. field staff.
Manuring
65.       The recommended applications based on soil analyses are as follows:

fertilizer trial results.
66.        In young plantings the fertilizers are broadcast on the whole sur-
face of the weeded circle; this is also done in mature plantings unless the
interrow is sufficiently clear of plant debris for the fertilizer to be spread
mechanically over the whole surface. The dressing should be given in April at
the beginning of the rainy season, before the heavy rains set in.

NIGERIA
NUCLEUS ESTATE/SMALLHOLDER OIL PALM PROJECT
RIVERS STATE
ROAD IMPROVEMENT
Introduction
1.        Rivers State has a communication system in which all transport modes
are represented. There are 6,300 km of roads, 40 km of railways, numerous
navigable rivers and a domestic airport at Port Harcourt. Surface transport
is governed by the State's geography and by the presence of Nigeria's second
most important harbour, Port Harcourt, in the southern part of the State.
Thus, the main State transport lines consist of the navigable channel on the
Bonny River to Port Harcourt, and the trunk roads and railway lines radiating
from Port Harcourt towards Cross River and Imo States.
2.        The road network is predominant in the north, the coastal area being
heavily dependent on river navigation. There are 380 km of Federal trunk
roads, 2,900 km of State roads and 3,000 km of Local Government Authority
roads. Presently, however, division of responsibility for roads between
Federal, State and Local Governments is unclear. The Federal Government
constructs, but does not maintain, the trunk roads system; the State is re-
sponsible for construction and maintenance of the State roads and (with funds
provided by the Federal Government) maintenance of the Federal trunk road net-
work. A Federal Road Maintenance Center is planned for 1980, to relieve the
State of responsibility for maintaining the Federal roads. Local Government
Authorities are responsible for maintaining local roads and tracks.
3.        A 1976 reorganization of local government replaced the former Divi-
sions with larger units, Local Government Authority (LGA) areas. These will
henceforth receive a considerable increase in Federal and State funds and
local government will be increasingly responsible for local roads and tracks.
4.        A considerable road construction program is planned in the State
for the current development plan period (1976-1980). In addition to a Fed-
eral program for reconstruction and/or upgrading of about 60 km, the State
government plans a construction program costing N 39.2 million, covering 223
km and representing 22% of the development plan budget.
Communications in the Project Area
5.        The smallholder project area, centered about 40 km north of Port
Harcourt, covers 2,070 km2 or 12% of the land area of the State. It contains
164 km of existing asphalt roads and about 1000 km of earth roads and tracks.
There are virtually no gravel roads due to the lack of suitable gravel mate-
rial in the area. The main communication lines from the south, west and north



ANNEX 4
Page 2
of the project area are satisfactory, but the east part is deprived of all-
weather roads and is cut off from the center (where the oil mills will be
situated) by the Otamiri and Ogochia Rivers which are presently not bridged.
6.        The terrain is very flat, with soils consisting of fine to medium
grained sand with a clay content from 5 to 20%; permeability is high. Some
swampy areas occur in the south-west, the remainder being without any topog-
raphic features.
7.        The asphalt road network is generally satisfactory, but earth roads
are nearly all in poor condition. The principal reasons are (i) absence of
proper surfacing material and insufficient grading which produce heavily
rutted surfaces; (ii) insufficient clearing along the roads permitting the
drip-line of trees to reach the carriageway; (iii) insufficient and badly
maintained longitudinal and offshoot drains; (iv) insufficient superelevation
of the carriageway; and (v) the universal absence of a proper crown on the
carriageway to insure run-off of rainwater to the side ditches. The earth
roads are frequently impassable by motor vehicles during the rainy season,
a condition not acceptable to the project. The high density (0.5 km/km2)
of the earth road network is thus not an indicator of the adequacy of com-
munication within the rural parts of the project area. Bridges are, except
on the recently constructed trunk roads, mostly temporary structures erected
to alleviate damage from the 1967 civil war.
Project Road Component
(i) Selection
8.        All smallholder planting will be within a maximum distance of 600 m
on either side of the roads on which fruit collection is organized. This is
expected to result in 12 ha/km of planting along existing asphalt roads, and
20 ha/km along new roads upgraded to all-weather standards during the project
period.  This determines the road component of the project required to serve
the 10,000 ha of smallholdings to be planted:
Existing asphalt roads
165 km                                   2,000 ha
Planned asphalt roads
78 km                                   1,000 ha
Earth roads improved or constructed
under the project
350 km                                    7,000 ha
Link roads to oil mills
(outside the project area 50 km)
Total                       10,000 ha



ANNEX 4
Page 3
9.        The selection criteria for road to be included are (i) an equitable
distribution of project benefits, avoiding concentration along existing lines
of communication; (ii) economy of road works, avoiding costly swampland and
river crossings whenever feasible. Maintenance of improved or constructed
roads would form part of the project during the 4-year construction period.
10.       The network to be established contains 210 km of new construction
and 190 km of road improvement works (see map). In addition, a temporary
bridge over the Ogochia River at Umuaturu is included as no firm timing is
available from the State Government for construction of a permanent bridge
(to be part of the planned east-west road).
(ii) Design
11.       Design standards are outlined in Figure 1.  The carriageway would
be one lane 4 m wide, with a 1.2 m shoulder and a ditch on either side. Prim-
ary design considerations are:
-    satisfactory drainage, featuring (i) a profile crown of not
less than 15 cm to insure run-off from the carriageway to
the longitudinal ditch; (ii) continuous longitudinal ditches
not less than 50 cm deep; (iii) off-shoot drains and culverts
adequate for transporting and disposing of flash flood water;
(iv) removal of the tree drip-line from the carriageway and
shoulders;
-    adequate compaction to stand up to the foreseen truck axle
loads (about 7 tons).
12.       The terrain and topography present no obstacles to these require-
ments. Traffic volumes are not expected to exceed 20 vehicles per day, but,
in view of the terrain, a design speed of 40 km per hour is recommended.
The flat terrain in the project area does not warrant detailed design work
for the longitudinal profile and cross sections, but after staking the
centerline the slope of roadside ditches and the position and dimensions
of offshoot drains should be established in tabular form from reconnaissance
on the spot. Although the soils are in general permeable and the length of
offshoot drains thus moderate, the drains would have to be tightly spaced
to avoid excessive depth of the side ditches; the average distance between
the drains would be about 60 m, with an effective area of absorption of about
15 m2 each.
13.       Surfacing with gravel of other selected material is not considered
necessary, in view of the low traffic volume expected and the high cost of
material, which would have to be transported over prohibitive distances from
quarries outside the Rivers States.
14.       Culverts would be designed in concrete pipes, 80 cm in diameter,
with concrete head walls. In the area east of the Sambreiro River, the need
for culverts is modest, about one for every km of road, whereas west of the
river it increases to more than one per km. A total of 375 culverts are
included in the program. The bridge on the Ogochia River would be a standard



ANNEX 4
Page 4
temporary bridge (Bailey bridge or equivalent) requiring no design work except
for foundations and abutments. The design work does not warrant engaging
staff, outside firms or individuals. All design work would be done by the
project's roads engineer, assisted by a team of surveyors.
(iii) Construction Methods and Costs
15.       The high cost of labor (about N 2.00 per day) rules out construction
by labor intensive methods (except where required to avoid destruction of farm-
land (offshoot ditch construction), and mechanical construction methods would
be used. The main construction unit would be the motorgrader, assisted by a
light bulldozer for clearing and stockpiling. Compaction would be done by a
self-propelled rubber wheel roller and a wheel-loader, and three light dump
trucks would provide material for embankments.
16.       The dozer would carry out clearing of bush, level the terrain and
remove the topsoil where required for grader access. Except for swampy areas,
the grader would then construct the cross section using the naturally occurring
soil materials by moving earth from ditches to the center of the road. The
roller would compact the carriageway section, and ditches would be rectified
by hand. Offshoot drains would ordinarily be cut by hand or, if the topography
and land use permit, by the grader. Embankments for swampland crossings would
be constructed by the dozer, wheel loader and dump trucks, fill material being
compacted in 15-20 cm thick layers by the roller.
17.       Concrete pipes for culverts should be placed on a cushion of sand-
fill and lean concrete, and the surrounding trench compacted by hand ramming.
The head walls should be simple rectangular protections in mass concrete.
The positions of the culverts should be marked by casting reflecting signs
about 50 cm high in the head walls.
(iv) Workshop
18.       To service equipment, the nucleus estate workshops would be used.
The first of these, built in 1977, would be fully occupied with estate equip-
ment maintenance by 1979. In that year, the second estate workshop would be-
come operational, and would for the next two years undertake repair of Road
Division equipment. The costs of this service are included in the road
component project costs.
(v) Work Volumes and Costs
19.       Required work volumes for improvement, construction and maintenance
work respectively are in Table 1, together with equipment requirements for key
units. An even spread for key units (Table 3) over the construction period
(4 years) determines the annual volume of construction, improvement and main-
tenance work to be carried out annually. The estimated work programs, which
are summarized below, are ahead of planting programs requirements:



ANNEX 4
Page 5
Year                           1978          1979       1980        1981       Total
Construction                    90             70         50          --        210
Improvement                     40             40         40          70        190
Maintenance                     --            130        240         330        700
Maintenance of improved or constructed roads form part of the project during
the 4-year construction period. Capital and operating costs of equipment are
given in Tables 5-7, with a costs summary in Table 1.
20.       Total costs of the roads program are estimated at N 1.3 million in-
cluding equipment, workshop costs, drainage works, maintenance expenditure on
equipment and personnel costs.
(vi) Execution
21.       There are no local contractors working on earth road construction,
and neither the State nor Local Government Authorities have satisfactory ex-
perience in building and maintaining earth roads. The road program would
therefore be carried out by a special unit, the Road Division, set up under
the Smallholder Management Unit. A road engineer with experience in earth
or gravel road construction and maintenance would be recruited to head the
Division. If no local qualified engineers were available, this position
would be filled by international recruitment. If so, a Nigerian engineer
would also be engaged and trained during the first two years of operation.
22.       Construction of the temporary bridge at Umuaturu which is included
in project costs would be done by State Ministry of Works contractors, with
payments being made directly by the State, against the Ministry's certificates
of completion.
23.       A cost accounting system should be established and operated by the
SMU in project costs during the entire works period, to define total and
partial costs of construction for improvement and maintenance, respectively.
(vii) Maintenance
24.       LGA's in the project area presently have no mechanized equipment
for road maintenance and all work is done by hand; the maintenance crews
have one laborer for every four kilometers. This would be adequate if LGA's
also had equipment for grading and occasional repair of embankments.
25.       Maintenance of roads constructed or improved under the project
would be the Road Division's responsibility until 1981, when responsibility
would be handed over to LGA's. The Road Division equipment, which at that
time will have a residual life of 2-3 years, would be handed over to the
LGA's on the understanding that it would be used for project roads and



ANNEX 4
Page 6
servicing of the equipment would continue to be carried out in the project
workshops, on a commercial basis. During the project period, LGA's should
second one or two foremen to project workshops for on-the-job training.

NIGERIA
NUCLEUS ESTATE/SMALLHOLDER OIL PALM PROJECT
RIVERS STATE
Grant/Credit Arrangement for Smallholder
Background
1.        In the Oil Palm Rehabilitation Scheme introduced in the early 1960s,
and continued until the outbreak of the civil war, smallholders received grants
comprising (a) N 100 per ha in cash towards the cost of land clearing and com-
pensation for income lost, and (b) planting materials and fertilizers valued
at N 10 per ha. Under the three ongoing Bank-financed oil palm projects
(Bendel, Imo and Ondo States), the grant/credit scheme includes (a) a grant
for planting material, fertilizers and chemicals valued at N 170 per ha and
(b) credit in cash of N 100 per ha, for payment of labor.
2.        If Nigeria is to avoid becoming a major palm oil importer old wild
groves must be replanted with high yielding material. Past producer prices
for palm oil have been substantially below world prices, and the surpluses
accruing to the Marketing Boards have largely been used for development in
other sectors. Farmers have been unable to accumulate savings to replant
their farms and suffer loss of income when oil palms are removed, or other
crops displaced, to allow new planting or replanting.
3.        In previous rehabilitation programs, smallholders were allowed to
leave up to 20 palms standing until the palms came into bearing. In the pro-
posed project, all old trees would be removed before planting to avoid root
competition and reduced light which results from leaving old trees standing.
In addition, because of poor smallholder discipline and encroachment into
the root system of young palms, food crop intercropping during the first two
years of planting is not recommended. The introduction of these strict tech-
nical standards requires compensation for loss of income through the cash
element of the grant/credit package to ensure that participants respond.
Establishment Costs
4.        Estimated costs of smallholder clearing, planting and establish-
ment (end 1976 prices updated to reflect mid-1977 prices; fertilizers and
chemicals at full cost) are as follows:



ANNEX 5
Page 2
/1

Grant/Credit Terms
5.        Once accepted into the scheme, and provided he attained the husbandry
standards required, the farmer would receive the inputs needed to establish
and maintain his holding. This includes both cash (for hiring additional
labor) and materials, which account for 41% of total establishment costs over
Years -1 to 3.
6.        For accounting simplicity and comprehension by the farmer, materials
for planting would be in the form of grant and the cash advance would be medium
term loan. Of the N 558.60 grant/credit 'package' offered, about 38% would be
given as grant and the balance as loan; the combined grant/credit would cover
83% of estimated total establishment costs, the remainder (part of the labor
required and hand tools) being provided by smallholers.
