/1   Fertilizers and chemicals fully costed.
7.        The estimated phasing of the cash assistance reflects labor required
and income loss offset and/or incentive needed before the smallholders palms
come into bearing. Part of the payment in Year 0 could be used by smallholders
for 'retroactive financing' of labor hired for clearing, and N 50 per ha would
therefore be paid early in Year 0 or just before planting. All cash payments
or supply of materials would be subject to a field inspection report verifying
that acceptable husbandry standards had been attained.
8.        The credit would be at 9.5% interest for 13 years from planting,
including a 7-year grace period during which interest would be capitalized.
Smallholder loans including capitalized interest would total N 510 per ha
after 7 years. Equal annual installments over 6 years would be N 115 per ha;
for simplicity, repayment would be deducted (at a monthly rate equivalent to
N 9.00 per ha) from individual smallholder fruit bunch sales to the mill.
9.        The loan terms proposed would provide a satisfactory net return per
ha to smallholders. Assuming a farmgate price of N 40.0 per ton of ffb, the
gross return (in constant 1976 terms) from 2 ha would start at N 254.0 in the
first year of production (Year 4) rising to N 578.0 during the farmer's loan
repayment period (Years 7-12) and thereafter would average about N 814.0.
These returns compare favorably with those obtainable from alternative tree
or food crops even where improved management and planting material are used.
A 2 ha planting would require about 60 mandays a year for maintenance and har-
vesting, and gross returns per manday would rise rapidly from N 3.9 in the



ANNEX 5
Page 4
year of first production (Year 4) to an average N 8.6 during years 7-12 of
loan repayment and N 12.9 thereafter.
10.       The majority of smallholders involved in oil palm cultivation also
grow food crops. The introduction of credit would establish an institutional
framework which could also be used to provide participating farmers with other
credit needs, e.g. food crops, using palm fruit sales income as collateral.
Once smallholders had established palms, SMU would aim to hand over the pro-
vision of short-term credit for regular inputs to cooperatives.
Arrangements for Grant/Credit Distribution
11.       SMU would be responsible for providing materials and cash to small-
holders. To be eligible for project grant/credit assistance, smallholders
must be members of a (registered) cooperative society. SMU staff would ap-
praise applicants for technical suitability and assess their credit worthi-
ness after consultation with the cooperative society. Applications would be
reviewed and approved by a Planting Authorization Committee (PAC) composed
of the General Manager as Chairman, the Controller of Field Operations, the
Financial Controller and a representative of the cooperative societies.
12.       Successful applicants would sign a loan agreement with their coop-
erative and SMU, in accordance with Federal and State legal requirements and
acceptable to the Bank. The Loan Agreement would stipulate the amount, terms,
conditions and schedule of loan repayments and would include an undertaking
by the smallholder to sell his fresh fruit bunches (ffb) to the central pro-
cessing mill.
13.       The grant/credit assistance would be limited to a minimum of 1
ha and a maximum of 10 ha per farm family. Delivery of materials for planting
would be made directly to smallholders by SMU vehicles. The procedures
involved in grant/credit distribution are summarized in Appendix 1.
14.       Credit facilities would be provided through SMU in conjunction with
registered cooperatives since an institutional framework for agricultural
credit is not yet sufficiently established in the project area. In addition,
both the small scale of the credit operation relative to SMU's other costs,
and the close relationship to SMU technical supervision make it desirable
for credit disbursement, accounting and recovery to be handled by SMU. The
smallholders' loan accounts would be integrated with the accounts and statis-
tics for the grant scheme. Since the additional cost of maintaining loan
accounts would be small, staff and equipment costs would be borne by SMU
(see Appendix 2).
Arrangements for Credit Collection
15.       Registered Cooperative Societies (RCS) would be used for credit
collection, and participation in the project smallholder scheme would be
restricted to RCS members for the following reasons:



ANNEX 5
Page 5
(a) they offer the best assurance of individual credit worthi-
ness;
(b) they provide a mutual guarantee of loan repayment, since
it is not feasible to rely on distraint of land or property
for loan recovery;
(c) by involvement in the project, the cooperatives would be
given direct responsibility to represent smallholders' in-
terest on the Steering Committee, the Planting Authoriza-
tion Committee (PAC), and the Price Policy Committee, all
of which would include State Cooperative movement repre-
sentative;
(d) involvement in the project would act as a stimulus to fur-
ther development of community/cooperative group activities
in smallholder agriculture; and
(e) payment to societies of a margin in recognition of their
loan repayment guarantees should benefit them financially
(provided their members maintain a satisfactory rate of
repayment) (see Appendix 3).
16.       The Risonpalm processing mills would provide SMU with a biweekly
summary of bunch purchases and a check for the full value. The SMU Account-
ing Unit would check this against its own bunch purchase slips (bps) and
analyze by cooperative society and individual smallholder, to determine the
loan repayments due. SMU would then credit each society's account with the
value of its members' bunch sales during the previous month, less credit
repayments due calculated on the basis of an interest rate of 7.5% (N 8.0
per ha planted per month over Years 8-13).
17.       On receipt of these funds each society would pay net amounts owing
to members after deduction of credit repayments at 9.5% (N 9.00 per ha planted
per month over Years 8-13).
18.       The procedures for credit repayment are summarized in Appendix 4.



ANNEX 5
Appendix
Page 1
NIGERIA
NUCLEUS ESTATE/SMALLHOLDER OIL PALM PROJECT
RIVERS STATE
Smallholder Grant/Credit - Distribution (Years 1 to 3)
1.        Smallholder applies to join scheme.
2.        SMU inspects holding, consults with cooperative society on credit
rating.
3.        If Eupatorium infestation covered more than 30% of the area to be
planted then SMU shall assist the smallholder in clearing this
weed by providing spraying facilities the cost of which shall be
charged to the smallholders  credit account.
4.        If accepted, the smallholder, cooperative and SMU sign three-party
Loan Agreement (including Promissory Note).
5.        PAC authorizes planting (1-10 ha per family).
6.        SMU issues first cash payment (N 50 per ha) after clearing and
delivers first materials for planting.
7.        SMU continues to supply materials as appropriate, subject to
acceptable progress reports and regular inspection from field
assistants.
8.        Each smallholder's participation in scheme reviewed annually by
PAC (based on inspection reports by field assistants) and ter-
minated if necessary, after adequate warnings have been given.
9.        On satisfactory annual review by PAC, SMU issues annual cash
payments in arrears.
10.       After the establishment period, when plantings start producing
at least 4 tons/ha in Year 4, SMU makes available seasonal inputs
(viz fertilizer) on short-term credit to smallholder, if required.
Loan Agreement endorsed by smallholder, cooperative and SMU. Short
term credit probably not required after year 8, when full yield
attained.

Grant/Credit Accounting Control for Smallholders
1.        3SMU would have to maintain separate accounts for individual small-
holders (analyzed by cooperative society) to record the following basic in-
formation:
(a) disbursement of grant components in kind during the estab-
lishment period;
(b) disbursement of medium-term credit in cash during the
establishment period;
(c) disbursement of seasonal credit in maturity (Year 4 from
planting);
(d) weight, grade and value of fresh fruit bunches sold to
the mill by dates;
(e) deductions made for repayment of medium-term and seasonal
credit;
(f) block payments to cooperatives.
2.        SMU would have to maintain smallholder accounts, each requiring 4-5
entries per year during the development period and 25 per year when harvesting
commences. Since the recording requirements would be too numerous to be done
manually, provision would be made for mechanical recording on individual cards
using simple accounting machines each capable of registering about 10,000
entries per month. These machines can be operated by a specially trained
typist. Based on an average farm size of 2 ha, 5,000 smallholder accounts
would be handled requiring 2 accounting machines.

Financial Effect on Cooperatives
1.        On the proposed phasing of distribution and repayment of the small-
holder loans of N 300 per hectare, repayments due from the smallholder to his
cooperative would be N 9.00 per hectare per month over 72 months, based on an
interest rate of 9.5%. If the cooperatives were charged interest at a rate of
only 7.5% in recognition of their guarantee of loan repayments by their mem-
bers, then their monthly obligation to SMU in respect of members' planted area
under the scheme would amount to only N 8.00 per hectare per month. Thus with
no default on members' repayments to the cooperatives the Societies would gain
a margin of N 1.00 per month or N 12.00 per year over the 6-year repayment
period.
2.        This margin would be sufficient to cover current repayment obliga-
tions to SMU with a rate of default or arrears of up to 11.1% of members' own
repayments. Net margin (loss) per hectare to cooperatives at different rates
of default/arrears are shown below:
Percent default/arrears on
smallholder repayments to            Net margin (loss to Cooperative
Cooperatives                    Naira per hectare per year) /1
0                                     12.00
5                                      6.60
10                                      1.20
11                                      0.12
11.1                                    0'.00
15                                     (4.20)
20                                     (9.60)
/1   The net margin for any intervening or higher rate of default can be
calculated as follows y = 12 - 108. x;
y = net margin (loss)
12.00 = annual margin to cooperatives at no default (108 - N 96.00 =
N 12.00)
108 = annual repayments by smallholder to cooperative (N 108.00)
x = percentage default.
Thus, provided that members' early repayments performance was reasonably satis-
factory (default of not more than say 5%) the cooperatives would fairly soon



ANNEX 5
Appendix 3
Page 2
accumulate surpluses to their account sufficient to tide them over any tem-
porary decline in members' bunch sales to the mill, due for instance to sea-
sonal low yields, and allow them to maintain full payment of their obligations
both to SMU and to growers. The importance of regular and prompt cash payments
to the smallholders is one of the main reasons for maintaining a 2% interest-
spread in favor of the cooperatives.
3.        Based on the smallholder planting program for the proposed project,
Table 1 summarizes the annual and cumulative effect of the credit arrangements
on the cooperative movement's finances, on assumed default rates of 0, 5, 10
and 11% (11.1% default marks break-even point for cooperatives).
4.        Table 1 shows that even with default on smallholder repayments of
5% the cooperative should accumulate sizable cash surpluses as a consequence
of the margin allowed to them on credit repayments to SMU. By the time annual
repayments reached their peak (project year 12), they would be in a position
to have accumulated a cash reserve approximately equal to two months' repay-
ments due to SMU. With satisfactory repayment performance by smallholders,
surpluses over and above a prudent level of reserves would be available for
financing of seasonal credit requirements or distribution to members whose
accounts were in good standing.

Smallholder Grant/Credit - Repayment
(Medium-term: Year 8 to 13, short-term: Year 4 et seq.)
1.        Collection system (mill) buys ffb from smallholder.
Copies of bunch purchase slip (bps) to:
(1) smallholder
(2) SMU
(e) mill
2.        Mill sends SMU two-weekly tabulation of bps and check for full
amount without deductions.
3.        SMU (Accounts Unit) checks total against own copies of individual
bps and analyses by cooperative coding.
4.        SMU posts credits to respective cooperative accounts with  SMU.
5.        From individual smallholder cards, SMU knows monthly repayment due
from members of each cooperative; debits this amount - based on N 8.00 per ha
(interest charged at 7.5%) - against each cooperative account.
6.        Check for net amount issued direct to cooperatives bank account;
cooperative supplied with schedule of individual member's repayments due -
based on N 9.00 per ha (interest charged at 9.5%) - and net amount due to
member.
7.        Schedule is balanced with check payment from SMU by credit entry for
interest spread on repayments due.
8.        Cooperative pays out its members in cash, net of repayments at 9.5%
(N 9.00 per ha); members can check own bps against cooperative's schedule.
9.        Any member failing to send sufficient ffb to mill to cover monthly
repayment would owe his cooperative; this debt would be shown on his individ-
ual card at SMU and as a debtor's item in his cooperative account at SMU,
and carried forward to the next month.



ANNEX 5
Appendix 4
Page 2
10.       Short-term credit if any would need to be accounted separately from
the medium-term loans but handled in the same way, on a flat rate monthly de-
duction from fruit sales with a margin to cooperatives in respect of repayment
guarantees.
11.       With interest spread on credit of 7.5-9.5% the margin to cooperative
is sufficient to cover non-repayment/arrears equal to over 11.1% of monthly
debt to SMU.
12.       Even if arrears temporarily exceed 11.1%, 1/ cooperative can still
pay out its members in good standing in full by meeting the difference out
of accumulated reserves, provided that initial repayments record is satisfac-
tory (allowing cooperative margin to accumulate).
1/   E.g., as a result of seasonally low bunch yields.

Fruit Collection System
General
1.        At full maturity in 1990 there would be about 250,000 tons to be
delivered annually to the mills for processing. During the peak production
season daily delivery from smallholders would be about 600 tons and from
estates 800 tons. The collection system is essentially the same for
smallholdings and estates.
Collection Systems
2.        Harvested ffb would be transported to roadside pickup points by
farmers and by harvesting teams in the estates. The maximum distance "head
carried" to the pickup point would be 600 m. From the pickup point to the
mills, average trucking distance is estimated at 20 km for the smallholdings
and 8 km for the estates.
3.        The trucks would be equipped with high-sided rear-tipping platforms
to accommodate up to 7.5 tons of ffb which would be placed on nets spread on
the ground at pickup points ready for loading. A crane would be mounted be-
hind the truck cab, equipped with a built-in scale to weigh the ffb when
loading.
4.        Collection nets should be made from synthetic fiber impregnated
against the effects of sunshine. The mesh should permit simultaneous loading
of loose fruits and bunches, and should not be larger than 2 cm x 2 cm.
5.        It is estimated that each truck serving the smallholder area would
make 3 trips a day during peak harvesting; for the estate each truck would
make 6 trips. On this basis the truck requirement at full production (1990
onwards) would be 9 for each estate and 27 for the smallholdings.
6.        The nets for smallholder collection from the smallholdings would
be distributed to farmers by the project - one net for every two ha - who
would be responsible for the nets. On the estates, nets would be distributed
by collection trucks on collection day.
7.        The trucks would be manned by a driver and two laborers to handle
nets and loading. Smallholders would be paid according to the weight and
quality of ffb weighed at pickup points. The truck driver would be respon-
sible for weighing and classification of fruit quality, and would issue
farmers receipts for delivery.



ANNEX 6
Page 2
8.        The fruit collection system would ultimately require a staff of 45
drivers, 90 laborers, two clerks and an accountant. It would be headed by
a transport manager with Lwo a6siscants, one for each mill, and would require
close cooperation between the transport manager and mill management.
9.        At full production, recurrent ffb collection costs would be about
N 375,000 annually. Renewal of trucks is estimated to occur after 4 years for
trucks serving smallho'lders, and 6 years for those serving estates. Nets are
estimated to last 4 years for smallholdings where each net would be used once
a week, and two years in the estates, where nets would be in daily use. De-
tailed cost estimates including equipment renewal are in Annex 8, Tables 10
and 11. With one net per ha and 60 nets per truck on the estates, net require-
ment would total 6,080 at full maturity.

NIGERIA
NUCLEUS ESTATE/SMALLHOLDER OIL PALM PROJECT
RIVERS STATE
Preparation of a Coconut Project and Establishment of a Seed Garden
1.        As a result of plant breeding and other research over the past 20
years the coconut palm, under suitable conditions, can now yield around 5
tons of copra or 3.5 tons of oil per ha per annum; future selections are
expected to produce 7 to 8 tons of copra or about 5 tons of oil per hectare.
These high yields have been obtained from hybrid material using the yellow
or red Dwarf crossed with the West Africa Tall.
2.        The climate and soils of the tree crop belt in Nigeria provide
suitable conditions for this hybrid material and yields between 4 and 4.5
tons/ha could reasonably be expected. Compared with oil palm, the coconut
palm is less demanding in terms of soil and water deficit conditions, enabl-
ing development in areas where oil palm yields would be low. Planting and
harvesting are similar to those for oil palm cultivation and coconuts could
be readily introduced into the smallholder sector. Estimated returns to
investment (see Appendices 1 and 2) are comparable with oil palm.
3.        Coconut has a wide ranging end-use market, some of the more impor-
tant being:
-    dry kernel or copra from which oil can be extracted for
use in soap, margarine, biodegradable detergents, cosmetics,
pharmaceuticals, etc. - the residual dry matter from the
oil extraction processing being converted into cattle
feed;
-    fresh kernel which can be consumed in its raw state;
-    dessicated coconut for use in the confectionary trade;
-    the shells can be converted into charcoal for use as local
fuel or in antipollution heating systems;
-    the coir fibre extracted from the husk, which can be made
into ropes, carpets, brushes, etc.
Research is also being carried out to perfect the extraction of protein and
oil directly from fresh kernel.
4.        In view of the projected expansion in consumption of animal and
vegetable fats and oils during the last quarter of this century (annual con-
sumption is expected to rise from around 50 million tons in the mid-seventies



ANNEX 7
Page 2
to about 62 million tons in 1985 and possibly 70 million tons in the early
90's), coconut oil production will need to be expanded considerably to retain
its present 6% share of the market.
5.        Both the Federal and State Governments have expressed considerable
interest in smallholder and estate coconut development. Based on a Federal
Department of Agriculture recommendation, the project includes the financing
of (a) a feasibility/project preparation study and (b) establishment of a
coconut seed garden. The Rivers State Government has already provided for
development of a seed garden in its 1976/80 development plan.
6.        Feasibility/Project Preparation Study:  The objectives of the study
would be to:
(a) assess prospects for developing coconuts in the tree crop belt
of Nigeria;
(b) prepare specific investment proposals for smallholder and estate
coconut plantings in Southern Nigeria.
The main components of the study are:
(a) Comparative Advantage/Disadvantage assessment of coconut to
other tree crops in the tree crop belt of Nigeria including
oil palm, cocoa and rubber;
(b) Location of coconut development potential - within the Nigerian
tree crop belt generally and more specifically in different
States. The study should include identification of areas
suitable for (i) smallholder and (ii) estate development: in
both instances infrastructure development (roads, bridges, etc.)
required should be included. (At this stage proposals should
be reviewed with Federal and State Governments to determine and
agree on specific projects in various States). The second
stage of the study would then proceed as outlined in (c) below.
(c) Physical, Technical, Financial, and Economic Analysis for pro-
posed planting programs should be prepared including processing
and marketing facilities.
The study should also include organization and management proposals. SOCFINCO
Ltd. has already carried out studies on behalf of the Rivers State Government
but a more comprehensive study is required to cover the entire tree crop belt
of Southern Nigeria. It is estimated that such a study would require about
12 man-months and a suitably qualified consultancy firm should be engaged in
early 1978 prior to the establishment of a coconut seed garden (if any). The
cost of the feasibility study project preparation has been estimated at around
N 80,000 (US$134,000).



ANNEX 7
Page 3
Establishment of Coconut Seed Garden
7.        To develop a sizable coconut sector in the tree crop belt of Nigeria,
steps need to be taken to establish a source of high yielding planting mate-
rial. The feasibility/project preparation study discussed above would start
in early 1978 and if this proves to be viable a coconut seed garden could be
scheduled for initial planting in 1979.
8.        A 120 ha seed garden of hybrid dwarf and tall coconuts is proposed
under the project.  The seed garden would be near the oil palm Nucleus Estate
and managed by an experienced plant breeder assisted by a suitably qualified
and experienced field manager. The seed garden would comprise :
(a) 100 ha of Dwarf "mother trees" - 50 ha of Red Dwarf and 50 ha
of Yellow Dwarf;
(b) 10 ha of West African Tall pollinators;
(c) 10 ha of Tahiti and Rennell Pacific Tall pollinators.
Details of establishment and maintenance costs of the seed garden are in Appen-
dix I and Annex 8 (Table 12). The required planting material will have to be
ordered from the Ivory Coast, Benin and Malaysia. Table 1 gives details of
the type, number of trees required and source of planting material.

Farm Budget for Copra
10.       Appendix 2 shows a hypothetical farm budget for a smallholder cul-
tivating coconuts. Table 2 to the Appendix summarizes the results for com-
parison with the oil palm farm budget in Annex 12. Subsequent tables to
Appendix 2 detail input coefficients, processing costs for a small kiln
operating on the 'Trinidad Method', and derivation of a farmgate price
on the assumption that the copra is exported.
11.       Coconut palm requires a higher labor input than the same area of
oil palm, for two reasons: first coconut palms are planted closer together
than oil palm, and a larger number of trees requires maintenance; second,



ANNEX 7
Page 5
for copra, a lot of processing is done on the farm including nut splitting
and extraction of fresh kernel. Once trees reach maturity, pre-processing
requirements account for half of the total labor input.
12.       The farm budget in Appendix 2 Table 1 assumes adoption of a coconut
grant credit scheme similar to that for oil palm under the proposed project.
It shows that farmers might expect a higher return to land (both gross return
and net benefits) from coconut than from oil palm, but at the cost of a higher
labor inputs. The return to labor is comparable for both crops.
13.       Assuming that oil palm and coconut are not normally in direct com-
petition for the same land, the analysis indicates that coconut development
could be economically attractive in Rivers State, and strengthens the case
for diversification into copra production.

NIGERIA
NUCLEUS ESTATE/SMALLHOLDER OIL PALM PROJECT
RIVERS STATE
Project Management, Organization and Staffing
1.        Project implementation would involve:
Risonpalm Ltd. - which would be responsible for establishing and
operating the nucleus estate and oil mills, and a smallholder fruit
collection system.
The proposed Smallholder Management Unit (SMU) - which would be
responsible for executing the smallholder component, including
appraising farmers' applications and supervising technical and
financial services involved in field establishment and post-
maturity stages.
The Federal Department of Agriculture's Monitoring and Evaluation
Unit (MEU).
B. Risonpalm Limited
Background
2.        In August 1974, the Rivers State Agricultural Production and Mar-
keting Corporation (APMC) was established to take over the Rivers State Devel-
opment Corporation (RSDC) and the Rivers State Marketing Board (RSMB). APMC
absorbed the functions of these two statutory bodies which included the large
scale production of cash and food crops and the purchase of gazetted agricul-
tural produce (palm oil, palm kernel, cocoa, copra, etc.) from local farmers
in Rivers State through Licensed Buying Agents (LBA). In addition, it ad-
ministers the Elele oil palm and rubber estates (Elele, Abara and Odagwa).
After project implementation, the rubber plantations of APMC would be op-
erated by the Delta Rubber Co. Ltd., and the oil palm plantation, including
marketing and processing by Risonpalm Limited.
Risonpalm Limited
3.        Risonpalm Ltd., a company which was incorporated under the Companies
Decree 1968 was formed on November 24, 1975, and which commenced operations in
March 1977, would be responsible for development of the APMC oil palm planta-
tions and the estate component of the project. The Board of Directors would
be appointed by the Executive Council of River State and would include repre-
sentatives from the Ministries of Agriculture, Finance, and Trade and Economic
Development. The Board would be responsible for policy. Risonpalm's senior



ANNEX 10
Page 2
management for the nucleus estate would be provided by an international firm,
acceptable to the Bank, operating under a management agreement. The senior
management positions comprise a General Manager, Financial Controller, two
Field Managers and two Mechanical Engineers. Details of other senior and
intermediate level staffing requirements are shown in Table 1. On the com-
missioning of the first mill, senior staff required would include the Mill
Manager, Processing Engineer and Maintenance Engineer. Personnel recruited
for these senior posts would be acceptable to the Bank, and where suitably
qualified Nigerian were not available would first be recruited internationally.
The management agreement would specify that Nigerian personnel should be pro-
vided with training and on-the-job managerial experience to enable them to
fill senior managerial and technical positions at an early stage.
4.        Under the management agreement the nucleus estate would have a high
degree of administrative and financial autonomy. The estate would prepare
annual budgets using cost estimates in this report amended to reflect changes
in costs and estate development policies. These would be submitted to the
Board of Directors for approval. The estate would subsequently prepare
monthly cash flow statements, showing costs, revenues and additional capi-
tal requirements. The monthly statements would be reviewed by the Board of
Risonpalm, but estate management would operate on this basis unless informed
to the contrary by the Chairman of the Board.
5.        Based on the approved project estate budget, the State Government
would make annual allocations to cover its own contribution to project costs.
6.        The estate would:  (i) keep records to reflect all operations and
the financial position (in accordance with consistently maintained sound ac-
counting practices); (ii) have the accounts audited by auditors acceptable
to the Bank; (iii) submit copies of their audited accounts and the auditors'
report thereon to the Bank within four months of the end of each financial
year; (iv) cause the reports of the auditors to be of such scope and detail
as the Bank may reasonably have requested; and (v) furnish such other infor-
mation concerning their accounts as the Bank may reasonably require.
7.        The management of the nucleus plantation and palm oil mills would
also be responsible for establishing a regular fruit collection system to
cover the needs of both estate and smallholders. The fruit collection sys-
tem is discussed in Annex 6.
C. Smallholder Management Unit (S.M.U.)
General Objectives
8.        A Smallholder Management Unit (SMU) would be established to imple-
ment the smallholder replanting program. S1MU would have a high degree of
financial and administrative autonomy and would report to the Ministry of
Agriculture and Natural Resources (MANR).  A Steering Committee comprised



ANNEX 10
Page 3
of Government agency representatives would determine policy and approve work
programs and budgets. When establishing SMU, a consulting firm acceptable to
the Bank would be engaged to set up a mechanized accounting system to meet the
needs of the smallholder project. The same firm would be required to advise
on the training required for staff to operate the system.
9.        SMU headquarters would be in Ahoada with Regional Units at Ahoada,
Isiokpo and Okehi. SMU would be staffed with personnel seconded from the
Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources or recruited elsewhere in
Nigeria or internationally. SMU would appraise new planting/replanting
applications and provide technical and financial services in the field es-
tablishment and post maturity stages. SMU planting techniques and standards
would be agreed with the Federal Department of Agriculture's Monitoring and
Evaluation Unit (MEU) and Federal research institutions.
10.       The Steering Committee - Composition and Functions.  The Steering
Committee would comprise:
(i) Chairman - Permanent Secretary of Ministry of Agriculture
and Natural Resources;
(ii)  Permanent Secretary to the Ministry of Finance or his repre-
sentative;
(iii)  A senior representative of the Cooperative Division of the
Ministry of Rural Development and Social Welfares;
(iv)  A senior representative of Ministry of Trade and Economic
Development;
(v)  The General Manager of Risonpalm;
(vi)  The General Manager of the Nucleus Estate;
(vii)  A representative of the Federal Ministry of Agriculture;
(viii)  The General Manager of SMU - who would be Secretary; and
(ix)  The Chief Agricultural Officer of MANR.
The Chairman would be authorized to co-opt up to three additional nonvoting
members and during the road improvement period a Ministry of Works and Hous-
ing representative would be co-opted. A quorum would consist of 6 voting
members and in the event of a tie, the Chairman would have a casting vote.
11.       The Committee would meet quarterly, and its principal functions
would be to:
(i) determine policy;



ANNEX 10
Page 4
(ii)  appoint the General Manager, the Controller of Field Opera-
tions and the Controller of Finance, for which Bank concur-
rence would be required;
(iii)  review the organization structure (see Charts attached to
this Annex) of the SMU, including terms of staff employment;
(iv)  approve the annual work programs and budgets;
(v) review quarterly progress reports and approve any work pro-
gram amendments; and
(vi)  approve the annual report and accounts.
Management Structure and Staffing
12.       Management Structure.  The management structure is outlined in Charts
1 and 2, to this Annex, the main divisions being:
(i) headquarters staff directing and supervising technical and
financial services operated by the Regional Units; liaising
closely with MEU in evaluating ongoing and planning future
programs establishing training programs for staff and farmers;
(ii)  Financial control, including budget preparation, control of
smallholder grant/credit and fruit payment accounts, internal
audit and general administration; and
(iii)  Regional Unit staff responsible for distribution and appraisal
of applications, supervision of grant/credit services, providing
extension and other services to the farmers.
After Risonpalm processing mills establish a fruit collection system, the Re-
gional Unit staff would need to liaise closely with the mills to ensure that
smallholders both understood and utilized the system. Deductions for loan re-
payments would be made from the value of fruit purchases. SMU's Controller
of Finance would be responsible for calculating: (a) the loan repayments due
to the State Government; (b) the balance to be passed on to the cooperatives;
and (c) the balance to be distributed to individual producers.
13.       Staffing.  The smallholder component would require suitably quali-
fied and experienced staff, recruited internationally if necessary. Middle
level technical and administrative positions would be filled by professional
or senior technical grade officers recruited from within the State or from
other States in Nigeria. Lower level field and administrative staff would
be recruited from existing Government agencies or the private sector. De-
tails of the numbers and phasing of professional and technical staff require-
ments are given in Annex 10 Table 1.



ANNEX 10
Page 5
14.       The General Manager.  The Chief executive of SMU would be delegated
full responsibility for implementing the planting/replanting program. He would
be directly responsible to the Steering Committee through the Permanent Secre-
tary of the Ministry of Agriculture. The person appointed should have a pro-
fessional qualification in agriculture and at least 10 years experience, 5 of
which should have been in a senior administration position.
15.       The Controller of Field Operations - would be responsible for orga-
nizing and directing the technical aspects of the program, including nursery
work, appraisal of applications, supervision of planting and maintenance in
the immature and mature stages. He would also be responsible for organizing
in-service training for all levels of fields staff. The person appointed
should have had at least 8 years experience of tree crop planting (oil palm,
cocoa or rubber), preferably at a senior management level with a commercial
organization.
16.       The Controller of Finance - would be responsible for the general
administration of the SMU and all project financial controls and procedures.
This man would need to be a well qualified accountant with at least 8 years
experience at senior management level preferably in agro-industry.
17.       The  Roads Engineer - would be responsible for establishing the
Roads Division and directing its operations for the first two years of the
project. He would be a qualified Civil Engineer with at least 10 years ex-
perience in road construction, including some experience with earth roads.
He would have completed at least one assignment including work planning,
technical standards, supply management and awards involving equipment to a
value of at least US$200,000.
18.       The three Regional Unit Managers - would be in charge of the field
operations at regional level and should be Senior Agricultural Officers (SAO)
with about 3 years experience at that level. Preference would be given to
SAO's who had experience of modern oil palm planting techniques, but in any
event, these officers would be given in-service training courses at NIFOR.
Other professional, technical and administrative staff of the Regional Units
would mainly be recruited from Government services or directly from tertiary/
secondary level educational institutions. In order to equip such staff with
the specialized techniques required for implementing this project, appropriate
in-service training would be given at selected institutions.
D. Monitoring and Evaluation Unit (M.E.U.)
19.       Under the three existing Bank financed oil palm projects, a Monitor-
ing and Evaluation Unit 1/ was set up in Benin as a division of the Federal
Department of Agriculture. This unit is primarily concerned with (a) finan-
cial and technical monitoring of on-going projects; (b) evaluating their
1/   For more details of M.E.U. see Report 474a-UNI (Supplement 4).



ANNEX 10
Page 6
financial, economic and socio-economic impact; and (c) providing assistance
in planning and preparation of future development programs. M.E.U. has been
operating since September 1975 and would be responsible for monitoring the
Rivers State Nucleus Estate/Smallholder Oil Palm Project.
20.       MEU staff work closely with Federal research institutions and uni-
versity faculties. Consultants acceptable to FMG and the Bank will periodi-
cally be used to cover specific activities such as research, business manage-
ment, processing engineering and project preparation. The MEU will prepare
progress reports for submission to Federal and State Governments, and, where
appropriate, external financing agencies.
21.       Project financial control systems will be examined by NEU at least
every six months with particular attention to the procurement, timing and pro-
cedures; disbursement methods; grant/credit accounting, produce price informa-
tion, and control of credit repayments. Technical evaluation will concentrate
on assessing standards being attained in nurseries, field planting, mainte-
nance, harvesting/tapping, produce collection, and processing. To facilitate
monitoring it will be necessary to establish an agreed system for quantitative
assessment of work in relation to field practices as inspection and field
supervisory staff must apply the same criteria.
22.       Continuous evaluation of the economic and socioeconomic impact of
projects will be carried out in conjunction with institutions such as the
Nigerian Institute for Social and Economic Research (NISER). In addition to
measuring financial/economic benefits accruing to participating farmers and
companies, assessments will be made of changes in employment levels, business
activity, consumption patterns, participation in education and other economic
and socio-economic indicators of community well being.
23.       The MEU will also assist in the planning and preparation of future
development programs by providing project management units and oil palm com-
panies with the basic support services required such as (a) providing detailed
guidelines for project preparation, (b) ensuring that services required from
other institutions are made available for project preparation, and (c) attend-
ing Steering Committee and Board meetings.
24.       The Nigerian specialist staff required to monitor the tree crop
projects will be given opportunities to receive advanced training in selected
fields of study.  This will include their being attached to specific institu-
tions in neighboring West African countries and the Far East for projects
ranging from 3 to 4 months.
25.       The cost of establishing and operating M.E.U. was included under
the Mid-Western (now Bendel) State Nucleus Estate/Smallholder Oil Palm Proj-
ect (Ln 1183-UNI).

Pricing Policy for the Purchase of Fresh Fruit Bunches
Price Policy Committee
1.        A price policy commiLttee w-uld be appointed, comprising:
(a) Permanent Secretary of Ministry of Finance;
(b) Permanent Secretary of Ministry of Agriculture and
Natural Resources;
(c) Registrar of Cooperative Societies or his representatives;
(d) General Manager of Risonpalm Ltd. or a senior representa-
tive;
(e) General Manager of the Nucleus Estate component of the
project;
(f) General Manager of 'SMU - Secretary;
(g) Representative of thLe Oil Palm Commodity Board;
(h) Two representatives of smallholders;
(i) Such other members as the State Government may decide.
Chairing the Committee might be shared or alternated between the Permanent
Secretary, Ministry of Finance and the Permanent Secretary, MANR.
2.        The Committee would establish a formula for calculating the price
of fresh fruit bunches purchased from participating smallholders by Risonpalm
Ltd. The company would apply this formula to derive the actual purchase price
which would be announced once a month (or at some other agreed interval).
Risonpalm Ltd. would not be permitted to deviate from the formula without
prior approval of the committee. The following guidelines would be taken
into account by the committee in determining the purchase price formula.
Price Calculation
3.        The price paid per ton of ffb would be determined from the revenue
received for the palm oil and kernels produced from a ton of ffb, after allow-
ing for the costs of fruit collection and processing, including depreciation,



ANNEX 11
Page 2
taxes and financial charges. The financial charges would be adequate to pro-
vide a reasonable return on capital investment, sufficient to cover interest
charges, and margins for building up reserves and paying dividends. The re-
turn on capital would be measured as a percentage of net revalued fixed
assets plus working capital; this percentage would be agreed by the price
policy committee within a suggested 10%-15% range. Depreciation on fixed
assets would be taken on a straight line basis for short-lived assets, such
as those used for fruit collection; for longer lived assets, such as the
mill, it is suggested that a more appropriate basis would be to allocate
depreciation charges according to actual produce processed as a ratio of
total processing expected over the full life of the mill (this would avoid
placing unduly heavy depreciation charges on the low volume of throughput
expected in the earliest years of the mill). After subtracting these costs,
calculated on a per ton basis, from the revenue received from the sale of
one ton of produce, the price per ton of products net of all costs would
remain. This would be the base price.
4.        Applying the appropriate extraction rate of products to bunches to
this base price the price per ton of ffb could be calculated. Extraction
rates applied would be related to the mill's previous three months processing
results, with all participants receiving a price based on the overall average
extraction rate. While this would mean that producers with more mature trees
would be receiving slightly lower extraction assessment than might be justi-
fied, on balance the application of a standard extraction rate is considered
to be more practical from an operational standpoint.
5.        Any profits in excess of the agreed return on capital would be dis-
tributed to the smallholders in the form of a cash bonus at the end of each
half year. The amount paid to the individual smallholder would be propor-
tional to the quantity of fruit sold on the company over the period concerned.
Grading
6.        Having determined an ffb purchase price for Grade 1 fruit (suitable
stage of ripeness of tenera bunches) discount rates would need to be deter-
mined and agreed with producers to take account of:
(a) under- and over-ripe bunches;
(b) excessively long stalks;
(c) bunches with dirty fruits, stones, mud, etc.; and
(d) fruits from wild palm groves or dura material having a
lower oil content.
Since the mill would not have a captive market, prices offered would have to
be competitive with other bids prevailing in the area, and hence the minimum
price paid for ffb would be determined by prices prevailing in the local mar-
ket for a similar fruit. Thus it seems reasonable to assume that when deriving
a price per ton of ffb, the central mill could apply a combination of:



ANNEX 11
Page 3
(a) its own short-term selling price, say, a three-month average
from which it calculated a base price and hence an ffb price
per ton; and
(b) short-term trends in the local markets.
In the short-run, the mill might sustain losses in order to compete with local
market conditions. To anticipate changes in local market conditions, a monthly
index (adjustment factor) could be applied either to the base price or to the
ffb price calculated from the base price.

ANNEX 15
Page 1
NIGERIA
NUCLEUS ESTATE/SMALLHOLDER OIL PALM PROJECT
RIVERS STATE
Economic Implications
A.   Employment
1.        The project would expand employment opportunities in two categories:
(a) with SMU, the nucleus estate and mills, and the road building unit; (b) on
smallholder's farms, partly by family labor but also outside labor.
2.        The nucleus estate will be the largest source of employment, rising
to a peak of 1,760 in 1980, during the heaviest phase of the planting program,
finally, settling at about 1,500 when all palms reach maturity. At the peak
of the smallholders' planting program, in 1981, SMU would require a labor
force of 240-190 in the three regional units, the remainder at headquarters.
This would be cut back to 150 or less in the absence of a second phase proj-
ect. In the early stages building requirements for the nucleus estate, the
mill and SMU might create 75 jobs on average for five years and employment
opportunities on the roads component would be similar. Finally, the two
mills, when operating at full capacity in the late 1980's would employ a
labor force of 290. In summary, wage and salary employment resulting from
the project at full development would be about 2,000 jobs.
3.        In addition, the project would substantially expand employment oppor-
tunities on participating smallholders' farms; labor requirements per ha are
detailed in Annex 12. They are heaviest in the planting year when labor
input is 140 mandays per ha. At full maturity labor input for maintenance
and harvesting is lower, about 31.5 mandays per ha per annum. Many farmers
with 2 ha would be able to provide the 63 days input themselves, while con-
tinuing food crop production, though some wage labor would probably continue
to be used. In terms of full-time job equivalents, the total maintenance
and harvesting requirement for 10,000 ha would be around 1,200 man years,
but in practice this would probably involve a much larger number (the antic-
ipated 5,000 participating smallholders, and any outsiders working for them)
each working on a part-time basis. The benefits accruing to the smallholders
are shown in Annex 12, Table 1.
B.   Economic and Financial Prices
4.        Price calculations for oil and kernels are shown in Tables I and 2.
On the assumption that the oil produced will be a substitute for imports,
while kernels will be exported, prices are derived from the Bank's latest
international commodity price projections. In the economic analysis, a shadow
exchange rate was used to take into account the distorting effect of tarifs
and quotas on the international parity of the Nigerian Naira, and identifiable



ANNEX 15
Page 2
taxes were netted out. For cash flow purposes a price of N 308.40 per ton was
assumed. This price represents a compromise between the two extreme assump-
tions - either that imports would continue to be restricted altogether, or
that imports would be completely liberalized without any compensating adjust-
ment to the official exchange rate.
5.        The coconut seed garden was excluded from the calculation of the
economic rate of return for the project, since it does not represent an
integral part of the oil palm project. Seedlings are generally traded inter-
nationally in only fairly small numbers, and then propagated locally, reflect-
ing the fact that it would not be economic to implement a large-scale planting
program using imported planting material. However, using 'import substitution'
prices for seedlings (but without shadow-pricing foreign exchange) it is
estimated that the economic rate of return on the seed garden component alone
would be in excess of 30%. The financial rate of return would depend upon the
sales price of seedlings, as shown below:
Coconut Seed Garden
Sales Price of Seedlings (kobo)    Financial Rate of Return (Z)
40                               9.4
50                              12.6
60                              15.2
C.   Economic Rate of Return
6.        Project benefits are assumed to comprise the production of palm oil
and palm kernels. Pricing of these benefits is discussed in Section B above.
The project life is assumed to be 30 years with no residual value. Social
benefits resulting from the road improvement program have not been included
in the calculations.
7.        Project costs have been adjusted for the economic rate of retun
calculations as follows:
(i) price contingencies are omitted, but 5% physical contin-
gency is included throughout the project life;
(ii) identifiable indirect taxes and import duties are excluded;
(iii) the market wage for labor is assumed to reflect its opportunity
cost, in view of the fact that rural labor markets in the project
area are highly developed and a large share of agricultural work
is performed for money wages;



ANNEX 15
Page 3
(iv)  a shadow exchange rate of N I:US$1.27 is applied to output
prices and all foreign exchange inputs. The rate of return
is then recalculated using the official exchange rate of
N 1:US$1.54.
8.        Based on the above assumptions (including the shadow pricing
of foreign exchange) the economic rate of return of the project is 15.2%.
9.        The sensitivity of the economic rate of return to changes in
costs and benefits was tested. An increase of 10% in all costs, combined
with a decrease of 10% in all benefits, reduced the rate of return to 12.2%.
Without shadow pricing of foreign exchange the rate of return is 13.3%. For
streams of costs and benefits see Table 3.
